ABSTRACT

This report presents the results of a cultural resources survey for the proposed improvements to
the Pickering Basin and the development of the Port of Victoria Industrial Park South in southern
Victoria County, Texas. This investigation was conducted by Geo-Marine, Inc., under
subcontract to CivilCorp Engineers and Surveyors, for the Port of Victoria. The purpose of this
document is to provide data for use in the management of cultural resources in partial fulfillment
of obligations under National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA) of 1969 [42 U.S.C. §§ 4321–
4347; P.L. 91-190; 83 Stat. 852], the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of 1966, as
amended through 2000 [16 U.S.C. § 470 et seq.; P.L. 89-665; 80 Stat. 915], and the Antiquities
Code of Texas. These cultural resources investigations were conducted under Texas Antiquities
Permit No. 6812. Investigations consisted of a records search to identify previous cultural
resources surveys conducted in the area and the archeological and historic sites recorded by those
efforts, visual inspection of the project area, subsurface investigations via the excavation of
backhoe trenches and shovel tests, and archival research regarding historic-period resources
encountered within the project area. Fieldwork for this project was conducted in April and May
2012.
Thirteen archeological sites, 41VT155 41VT167, were documented and one previously recorded
archeological site, 41VT98, was revisited during this survey effort. In addition, the location of a
small rural cemetery (41VT168) was documented. Site 41VT98 has been previously determined
to be eligible for inclusion in the NRHP. This site extends into the proposed Industrial Park
South APE and is likely to preserve deeply buried cultural deposits. It is recommended that site
41VT98 be avoided by ground-disturbing activities or be subjected to additional testing prior to
ground-disturbing activities at the site. Site 41VT168, an unnamed historic period cemetery, is
also recommended for avoidance. Based on NHRP eligibility criteria, all remaining sites
encountered within the survey areas are recommended not eligible. Prehistoric site 41VT165 and
sites with prehistoric components (41VT156, 41VT158, 41VT166, and 41VT167) lack artifact
assemblages or intact cultural features that would retain sufficient data to contribute important
information regarding prehistoric technology, settlement-subsistence patterns, or extra-regional
interaction. The historic sites (41VT155, 41VT156, 41VT157, 41VT159, 41VT160, 41VT161,
41VT162, 41VT163, 41VT164, and 41VT167) and sites with historic period components
(41VT158 and 41VT166) either lack verifiable historical documentation and contextual integrity
or offer only redundant data, severely impeding their ability to yield significant information
iii

regarding historic period development and community patterns in Victoria County. All artifacts
and records associated with this project will be submitted to the Museum of the Coastal Bend in
Victoria, Texas, for curation.

iv

MANAGEMENT SUMMARY

This report presents the results of a cultural resources survey for proposed improvements to the
Pickering Basin and the development of the Port of Victoria Industrial Park South located
approximately 10.5 miles south of Victoria in southern Victoria County, Texas. Proposed
improvements to the Pickering Basin include the construction of oil dock piers, ramps, and
mooring dolphins, encompassing an area of potential effects (APE) of approximately 7.6 acres.
The proposed Industrial Park South encompasses an APE of approximately 1,390 acres, which
would be impacted by development of built-to-suit manufacturing and transportation facilities
and related infrastructure.
Due to the potential of the proposed Pickering Basin improvements and the development of the
proposed Industrial Park South to adversely affect cultural resources (i.e., archeological sites and
historic-era buildings and structures), this investigation was conducted by Geo-Marine, Inc.,
under subcontract to CivilCorp Engineers and Surveyors, for the Port of Victoria. The purpose of
this document is to provide data for use in the management of cultural resources in partial
fulfillment of obligations under National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA) of 1969 [42 U.S.C.
§§ 4321–4347; P.L. 91-190; 83 Stat. 852], the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of
1966, as amended through 2000 [16 U.S.C. § 470 et seq.; P.L. 89-665; 80 Stat. 915], and the
Antiquities Code of Texas. Intensive cultural resources investigations were conducted under
Texas Antiquities Permit No. 6812.
Cultural resources investigations consisted of a records search to identify previous cultural
resources surveys conducted in the area and the archeological and historic sites recorded by those
efforts, visual inspection of the project area, and subsurface investigations via the excavation of
backhoe trenches and shovel tests. Fieldwork for this project was conducted on 10 April, 25–27
April, and 5–17 May 2012. Thirteen new archeological sites, 41VT155–41VT167, were
documented, and one previously recorded archeological site, 41VT98, was revisited during this
survey effort. In addition, the location of a family or community cemetery (41VT168) was
documented. Two of the sites (41VT98 and 41VT165) were recorded as having exclusively
prehistoric components and eight (41VT155, 41VT157, 41VT159, 41VT160, 41VT161,
41VT162, 41VT163, and 41VT164) were identified as exclusively historic period sites.
Additionally, four isolates (Isolate 4, Isolate 10, Isolate 11, and Isolate 13) were exclusively
prehistoric in nature while 13 isolates (Isolates 1–3, Isolates 5–9, Isolate 12, and Isolates 14–17)
consisted of historic period items.
v

Site 41VT98 has been previously determined to be eligible for inclusion in the NRHP. This site
extends into the proposed Industrial Park South APE and is likely to preserve deeply buried
cultural deposits. It is recommended that site 41VT98 be avoided by ground-disturbing activities
or be subjected to additional testing prior to ground-disturbing activities at the site. Site
41VT168, an unnamed historic period cemetery, is also recommended for avoidance. Based on
NHRP eligibility criteria, all remaining sites encountered within the survey areas are
recommended not eligible. Prehistoric site 41VT165 and sites with prehistoric components
(41VT156, 41VT158, 41VT166, and 41VT167) lack artifact assemblages or intact cultural
features that would retain sufficient data to contribute important information regarding prehistoric
technology, settlement-subsistence patterns, or extra-regional interaction. The historic sites
(41VT155, 41VT157, 41VT159, 41VT160, 41VT161, 41VT162, 41VT163, and 41VT164) and
sites with historic period components (41VT158 and 41VT166) either lack verifiable historical
documentation and contextual integrity or offer only redundant data, severely impeding their
ability to yield significant information regarding historic period development and community
patterns in Victoria County.
Proposed improvements to the Pickering Basin and development of the Industrial Park South will
have no adverse effect any significant cultural resources, as defined under 36 CFR 60.4 and TAC
26.8. Provided the project is carried out as proposed, no further cultural resources investigations
are recommended. All artifacts and records associated with this project will be submitted to the
Museum of the Coastal Bend in Victoria, Texas, for curation.

CHAPTER 1
INTRODUCTION

This report presents the results of a cultural resources survey for proposed improvements to the
Pickering Basin and the development of the Port of Victoria Industrial Park South, located
approximately 16.9 kilometers (km 10.5 miles [mi]) south of Victoria, in southern Victoria
County, Texas (Figure 1). The proposed improvements to the Pickering Basin include the
construction of oil dock piers, ramps, mooring dolphins, and expansion of the Basin
encompassing an area of potential effects (APE) of approximately 7.6 acres (Figure 2). The two
proposed oil dock ramps measure approximately 24.7 meters (m; 81 feet [ft]) in length by 3 m (10
ft) in width. The depth of proposed impacts below the ground surface for ramp construction is
expected to be between 3.6 and 4.2 m (12 and 14 ft). The area projected for the potential
expansion of Pickering Basin is approximately 7 acres, measuring 172-x-165 m (565-x-540-ft).
The proposed Industrial Park South encompasses an APE of approximately 1,390 acres (Figure
3), which would be impacted by development of built-to-suit manufacturing and transportation
facilities and related infrastructure.
Due to the potential impacts of the proposed projects to cultural resource properties (i.e.,
archeological sites and historic-era buildings and structures), Geo-Marine, Inc., was contracted by
CivilCorp Engineers and Surveyors, to conduct an intensive cultural resources survey (GeoMarine, Inc., project number 30694.00.01) of the proposed project areas for the purpose of
recording and providing a preliminary evaluation of cultural resources within the APE, as
required by the National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA) of 1969 [42 U.S.C. §§ 4321–4347;
P.L. 91-190; 83 Stat. 852], the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of 1966, as amended
through 2000 [16 U.S.C. § 470 et seq.; P.L. 89-665; 80 Stat. 915], and the Antiquities Code of
Texas. Intensive cultural resources investigations were conducted in April and May 2012 under
Texas Antiquities Permit No. 6812. These investigations consisted of (1) background research
regarding previously recorded cultural resources within the APE and the vicinity; (2) backhoe
trenching adjacent to the Pickering Basin and within the floodplain portion of the proposed
Industrial Park South APEs to determine the potential for deeply buried archeological sites and to
provide geomorphological information regarding the study area; (3) pedestrian reconnaissance of
the project area; (4) systematic shovel testing along the Guadalupe River valley margin and
across the Pleistocene upland portions of the APE; and (4) archival research regarding historic
period sites recorded within the Industrial Park South APE. Fieldwork in the Pickering Basin
portion of the project area was conducted by Project Archeologist Ben Fullerton. Fieldwork in
1

the proposed Industrial Park South project area was conducted by Project Archeologist
Christopher Goodmaster and Field Technicians Cullom, Willi Hermann, Brett Lang, and Megan
Statham. Tanya McDougall conducted archival research focusing on the historic period resources
documented within the project area. Melissa Green served as the project Principal Investigator
throughout the process of this investigation. All artifacts and records associated with this project
will be submitted to the Museum of the Coastal Bend in Victoria, Texas, for curation.

5

CHAPTER 2
ENVIRONMENTAL CONTEXT

The proposed project areas are located along the eastern escarpment of the Guadalupe River
valley and the adjacent Pleistocene uplands in southern Victoria County, Texas. Situated within
the Western Gulf Coastal Plain, the topography of the project areas range from nearly level across
portions of the Pleistocene uplands and Guadalupe River floodplain to moderate slopes along the
valley escarpment. The soils of the project area derived from in situ weathering and development
of alluvial and eolian sedimentary deposits. This region is located within the Texan biotic
province (Blair 1950; Dice 1943) and supports a wide variety of flora and fauna.

GEOLOGY
Victoria County is located within the Western Gulf Coastal Plain physiographic region of Texas
(Fenneman 1938; Griffith et al. 2004). This region is characterized by nearly level upland plains
underlain by the unconsolidated sands and clays of the Pleistocene-aged Beaumont and Lissie
formations, whereas the Guadalupe River valley is infilled with clay-rich Holocene alluvium. A
comprehensive investigation of the geological setting encompassing the project areas (McGowen
et al. 1976) provides a detailed interpretation of the geological deposits that occur within the
lower Guadalupe River valley. In general, the project areas are situated within McGowen’s
Holocene–Modern Fluvial System, flanked to the east by deposits derived from a Pleistocene
Fluvial-Deltaic System and to the west by a Pleistocene Fluvial System (McGowen et al.
1976:Figure 4). The geological development of this region is concisely described by Abbott
(2001:12), in reference to an adjacent portion of the Western Gulf Coastal Plain, as representing
“a series of coalescent fluviodeltaic bodies arranged in an offlapped sequence, with interdigitated
and capping eolian, littoral, and estuarine facies making up a relatively minor component of the
lithology.” The influential force propelling this coastal development is the infilling of the Gulf of
Mexico from the Late Cretaceous period to the present, resulting in a series of generally parallel,
seaward-dipping sedimentary deposits transgressing toward the shoreline. The fluvial systems of
the region are largely controlled by glacioeustatic sea level changes, causing the lower reaches of
coastal-bound rivers to entrench their valleys during periods of low sea levels and deposit
sediments within prograding estuary environments created along the coastline during periods of
high sea levels. During intervals of higher sea level, floodplains were created along inland river
courses and result in fluvial terraces during subsequent periods of entrenchment during intervals
7

of low sea level. Fluctuations in sea level are also a driving force in channel meandering and
avulsion processes (Aslan and Blum 1999; Aslan et al. 2005; Makaske 2001; Slingerland and
Smith 2004; Stouthamer and Berendsen 2007; Taha and Anderson 2008). Subsequent reworking
of these sediments, largely by eolian processes during the Pleistocene and Quaternary periods,
has served to obscure evidence of the alluvial processes responsible for their initial deposition.
In the immediate vicinity of the project areas, the underlying geology consists of the Pleistoceneaged Beaumont Formation, one of a series of coastward-dipping fluvial and fluvial-deltaic
deposits that forms the generally level uplands of the Western Gulf Coastal Plain. Characterized
by clayey deposits, relatively well-preserved relict fluvial geomorphic features, prominent
vertisols, and well-developed gilgai microtopography (Nordt et al. 2006, Van Siclen 1985), the
Beaumont Formation is the youngest of these Pleistocene-age deposits, attributed to fluvialdeltaic processes during an interglacial period. Although vertebrate fossils have been recovered
from the Beaumont Formation, the most commonly documented fossils are marine and brackish
water invertebrates such as Rangia cuneata and Ostrea virginica (Deussen 1924; Sellards et al.
1932). The precise geological age of the Beaumont Formation is disputed (Aiuvalasit 2007;
Aronow 1988; Birdseye and Aronow 1991:A223; Blum and Aslan 2006; Blum and Price 1998;
Durbin 1999; Durbin et al. 1997; Gaston 1979; Heinrich 2007; McFarlan 1961; Nordt et al. 2006;
Otvos 1971); however, all estimates predate the earliest generally accepted commencement of
human inhabitation of the region. Thus, the potential for archeological site burial by Holocene
depositional processes is minimal, except in specific landscape settings.
Whereas the Beaumont Formation represents alluvial deposition during periods of relatively high
sea level during Pleistocene interglacial periods, alluvial deposition also occurred during periods
of falling and low sea levels associated with glacial periods. The best-preserved deposits of this
nature are series of alluvial features formed during the last glacial period that are inset into and
form terrace surfaces below the Beaumont Formation. First noted by Barton (1930), these
terraces were formally named the “Deweyville beds” by Bernard (1950) for deposits exposed
along the Sabine River in southeastern Texas. Deweyville terraces exhibit relict channel
dimensions considerably larger than modern river channels and are lower in elevation than the
Beaumont Formation but higher than the modern floodplains. Details regarding the extent,
origin, and significance of the Deweyville terraces has been the subject of considerable
speculation for some time, with Blum and Aslan (2006) providing the most comprehensive
discussion to date.
The Deweyville streams occupied valleys incised into the Beaumont Formation, and these
incision events essentially define the modern river valleys along the coast to this day. Blum and
Aslan (2006:191–194) note that the larger streams along the Texas coast exhibit at least three
suites of Deweyville landforms/deposits that represent streams that were graded to lower sea
levels than modern. The deposits of the Deweyville streams are significantly different from the
Beaumont Formation deposits in that they are predominantly coarse-grained, with fine-textured
sediments largely confined to lenticular channel fills. Laterally extensive vertical accretion facies
(fine-grained deposits) are rare, and in this regard, the Deweyville stream deposits present a stark
contrast to the Beaumont Formation alluvial deposits that are largely fine-grained. Dating of the
Deweyville deposits is ongoing, with the most recent studies suggesting ages ranging from ca.
58,000 to 14,000 years B.C.

8

Within the Guadalupe River valley, the Deweyville terraces have been examined by several
projects. In a paleogeographic study of the lower Guadalupe River valley, Weinstein (1992)
identified the older Deweyville 1 and a younger Deweyville 2 terrace that correlate to the
identification of a High Deweyville (i.e., Deweyville 1) and a Low Deweyville (i.e., Deweyville
2) in the proposed Victoria County Station project area (Tinsley 2010). The chronology of these
deposits was long debated (Blum et al. 1995), but recent dating by optically stimulated
luminescence (OSL) within the valley of the Guadalupe River (Aiuvalasit 2007) dated the High
Deweyville terrace to 50,000–60,000 years ago, and the Deweyville deposits at the Buckeye
Knoll site (41VT98) yielded OSL dates between 49,000 and 53,000 years ago. This correlates
reasonably well with a 52,000 date for High Deweyville terraces along the Nueces River (Durbin
et al. 1997). The ages of these deposits preclude the presence of archeological materials
preserved within intact deposits of Deweyville. However, archeological materials are routinely
recovered in the upper portions of Deweyville deposits (Abbott 2001) where these sediments
have presumably been reworked by eolian and/or slope processes, as they were at both the
Buckeye Knoll (41VT98) and McNeill Ranch (41VT141) sites. A recent study of site formation
processes in Deweyville deposits demonstrates that both localized secondary deposition during
the Holocene and bioturbation processes can bury archeological materials (Aiuvalasit 2007).
The Low Deweyville terrace forms a well-defined constructional alluvial surface below High
Deweyville remnants at the foot of the valley wall. This surface was often incorrectly mapped by
the Bureau of Economic Geology (1975) as Holocene alluvium and McGowen et al. (1976) do
not differentiate Deweyville terraces from Holocene-aged floodplain features (Tinsley 2010).
Unlike the High Deweyville remnants that lack surficial expression as a terrace, the Low
Deweyville deposits exhibit typical terrace morphology of level tread and scarp slopes. Along
the Nueces River, this terrace was identified as the Middle Deweyville and dated by
thermoluminescence (TL) and OSL to 40,000–41,0000 years ago (Durbin et al. 1997).

GEOMORPHOLOGY
The project areas occupy three distinct landscapes. Each of these landscapes contains a unique
suite of landforms with distinct geomorphic histories. The Guadalupe River floodplain in the
vicinity of the project area is relatively flat and underlain by fine-grained Holocene alluvium;
however, some fluvial features are still evident as microtopography. The Guadalupe valley
margin is a gently to moderately sloping escarpment of Deweyville and Beaumont terrace
deposits that have been dissected by several low-order ephemeral drainages. The Pleistocene
uplands occupy the generally level and featureless Beaumont Formation. With the exceptions of
efforts by Abbott (2001) and Weinstien (1992), few large-scale surveys of Gulf Coastal Plain
geomorphology, as it relates to the archeology of the region, have been conducted. Archeological
site contexts within the coastal plain vary significantly, with factors such as paleogeography,
alluvial sedimentation and erosion, eolian deposition, and colluvial deposition affecting the
patterns of past human habitation of the region and the integrity of the archeological record left
by those populations.
The Pickering Basin is located at the northern terminus of the Victoria Barge Canal, an artificial
waterway constructed in the late 1960s extending south along the Guadalupe River floodplain to
San Antonio Bay. The area proposed for Pickering Basin improvements occupies a heavily
modified portion of the upland escarpment adjacent to the Guadalupe River floodplain. Project
area elevations range from approximately 6 m (20 ft) above modern sea level (amsl) at the
9

location of the proposed oil dock ramps to 7–12 m (23–39 ft) amsl at the location of the Pickering
Basin expansion area (Figure 4). Prior to the construction of the Pickering Basin, this area was
the juncture of the Guadalupe River floodplain and a northwest-trending ridge of Deweyville or
Beaumont terrace deposits extending toward the floodplain. Blue Bayou occupied the floodplain
in this area prior to its channelization to the west of the Victoria Barge Canal.
The proposed location of the Industrial Park South expansion spans the Guadalupe River
floodplain, the terrace escarpment, and the uplands (Figures 5 and 6). The floodplain portion of
the project area is generally level and occupies an elevation range of 3.5–6 m (11.5–19.7 ft) amsl.
The Guadalupe valley margin, formed when the Guadalupe River occupied an arcuate channel
against the eastern valley wall during the Deweyville period (Weinstein 1991:Figure 2-7), has
been subsequently eroded into a gently to moderately sloping escarpment of 2–20 percent slopes
across an elevation range of 6–17 m (19.7–56 ft) amsl. Sandy deposits on the escarpment along
the western portion of the escarpment may represent the remnants of a High Deweyville terrace
or eolian deposits mantling the Beaumont Formation. A small, ovoid rise is evident along the toe
slope of the terrace escarpment and may represent a Low Deweyville terrace remnant. In recent
history, Black Bayou extended from the uplands in the eastern portion of the escarpment and
occupied a former channel of the Guadalupe River, feeding Atascosa Lagoon before flowing
south to Hog Bayou. Since the 1950s, Black Bayou has been dammed and channelized, changing
the drainage patterns of floodplain and obscuring the original channel. Atascosa Lagoon, which
once encompassed an area of more than 100 acres, is now represented by a wide, shallow
depression and a small stock tank of less than an acre in size to the southeast of the project area
boundary. The generally level upland portions of the project area occupy an elevation range of
17–20 m (56–65.6 ft) amsl and have been heavily modified by historic and modern agriculture.

SOILS
The proposed project areas are situated across seven soil series and one soil complex (see Figures
4 and 5). All information regarding the soils of the project areas has been synthesized and
summarized from data provided by the Soil Conservation Service (Miller 1982) and the Natural
Resources Conservation Service (NRCS 2012). Clay soils of the Trinity and Meguin series
dominate the floodplain portions of the project area, and the loamy sands and fine sandy loams of
the Kuy and Runge series mantle the valley margin. The uplands are largely composed of the
clay and sandy clay loam soils of the Laewest and Dacosta series; however, the fine sandy loams
of the Telferner series comprise a significant portion of the uplands within the Industrial Park
South project area. The undifferentiated Dacosta-Contee complex is a minor constituent of the
uplands. Typical soil profiles associated with each of these soil units are summarized in Table 1.

HYDROLOGY
The Western Gulf Coastal Plain landscape is traversed from northwest to southeast by the lowgradient alluvial valleys of the Frio, San Antonio, Guadalupe, Lavaca, Colorado, Brazos, San
Jacinto, and Trinity rivers. The proposed project areas are located within and adjacent to the
Guadalupe River valley, approximately 5 km (3.1 mi) downstream from the confluence of the
Guadalupe River and Coleto Creek. The Pickering Basin project area is situated approximately
2.1 km (1.3 mi) northeast of the modern Guadalupe River channel, whereas the Industrial Park
South project area is situated between 0.5 and 1 km (0.3 and 0.6 mi) northeast of the modern
channel. Prior to its channelization during construction of the Victoria Barge Canal, Blue Bayou

occupied the floodplain to the west of what is now the Pickering Basin. Atascosa Lagoon, a
naturally occurring water body that occupied an abandoned meander channel of the Guadalupe
River, extended into the Industrial Park South project area during the early period of European
settlement in the area. The lagoon was documented on early land plats of the area, but
channelization of local drainages and agricultural practices have largely drained the feature and
obscured its original location. Lin Lake, another natural water body occupying an abandoned
meander of the river, is located approximately 3.4 km (2.1 mi) southwest of the Industrial Park
South project area.
Several channels drain the upland escarpment within the Industrial Park South project area,
including one historically identified as Black Bayou. Many of these drainages have been
dammed to create stock tanks along the escarpment and channelized within the floodplain.
Likewise, two unnamed drainages spanning the upland portion of the Industrial Park South
project area have been extensively modified through channelization and modern agricultural
practices.

FLORA
The vegetation patterns within the project areas have been severely altered from their natural
states by modern agriculture and industrialization. Aerial photographs of the project areas
indicate that the majority of the area has been timbered and converted for use as cropland,
pasture, and industrial development during the twentieth century. In addition to modern
agricultural and industrial land use, other vegetation types were observed in the project areas.
Several areas composed of upland savannah-like characteristics exhibited intermittent dispersal of
dense live oak (Quercus virginiana) mottes, with mustang grapes (Vitis mustangensis)
encroaching many of the mature trees, and ground cover dominated by stinging nettle (Urtica
dioica) and bluebonnet (Lupinus subcarnosus). In some areas, honey mesquite (Prosopis
glandulosa), huisache (Acacia farnesiana), and sugarberry (Celtis laevigata) dominate the
hardwood uplands, with Cherokee rose (Rosa laevigata) encroaching on many of the mature
trees. Hardwood bottomlands within the Industrial Park South project area are dominated by
pecan (Carya illinoinensis), sugarberry, cedar elm (Ulmus crassifolia), chinaberry (Melia
azedarach), and huisache, with mustang grape, palmetto (Sabal spp.), and Cherokee rose ground
cover. A significant portion of the Industrial Park South project area is covered by immature
huisache and honey mesquite brushland, likely because these areas were agriculturally abandoned
in the recent past.

FAUNA
The Western Gulf Coastal Plain supports a wide variety of fauna. Mammals include whitetail
deer (Odocoileus virginianus), beaver (Castor Canadensis), armadillo (Dasypus novemcinctus),
raccoon (Procyon lotor), opossum (Didelphis marsupialis), gray fox (Urocyon cinereoargenteus),
and rabbit (Sylvilagus spp.). Birds residing in the area include herons (Ardea spp.), ducks (Anas
spp.), geese (Branta spp.), turkey vultures (Cathartes aura), hawks (Accipiter spp.), and owls
(Strigidae spp.). Several species of snakes, lizards, frogs, and turtles are among the reptiles and
amphibians living within the region. Some of the aquatic species native to the area are gar
(Lepisosteus spp.), gafftopsail catfish (Bagre marinus), eel (Anguilla spp.), clam (Rangia
cuneata), eastern oyster (Crassostrea virginica), and several mussel species (McGuff 1978;
White et al. 1989).
15

CLIMATE
The modern climate across the Western Gulf Coastal Plain is a function of proximity to the Gulf
of Mexico. The climate of the region is Subtropical Humid (Bomar 1995; Larkin and Bomar
1983), characterized by warm to hot and dry summers and mild winters. Temperatures are
modulated by southeasterly, warm, moisture-laden winds from the Gulf of Mexico. Cold, highpressure Canadian air masses from the north result in cool weather and rainfall across the region
in winter (Miller 1982). Average low temperatures range from approximately 15–21° Celsius (C;
59–70° Fahrenheit [F]), while average high temperatures range from 27–29° C (81–84° F).
Precipitation across the region is generally bimodal, with peaks occurring in the fall (September–
October) due to tropical disturbances and occasional hurricanes in the Gulf of Mexico, and as
slow gentle rains and thundershowers in the spring (April–June).

16

CHAPTER 3
CULTURAL CONTEXT

INTRODUCTION
This chapter presents a brief synopsis, based on regional archeology, of Native American cultural
chronology and Euro-American history of the central Texas coast culture area. Given the size of
the region and the depth and breadth of cultural complexity that has developed in the area, this
discussion must necessarily be limited in scope. References are provided to supplement the
information summarized here.

NATIVE AMERICAN CULTURAL CONTEXT
The central Texas coast culture area is typically defined as extending from the Colorado River
south to the northern reaches of Baffin Bay (Ricklis 2004). The following brief discussion of the
prehistoric archeological record of the region draws from previous summaries by Black (1989),
Campbell (1960), Ricklis (1995, 2004), and Weinstein (1992).
Thomas N. Campbell (1960) first synthesized the prehistoric archeology of the central and lower
Texas coasts, creating a two-part chronology: the pre-ceramic Archaic Aransas focus, followed
by the Late Prehistoric Rockport focus using artifact typologies and apparent stratigraphic
relationships. Campbell’s taxonomic designations, however, were created without chronometric
information derived from radiocarbon dating. In the decades since Campbell’s synthesis, there
has been a significant increase in archeological research efforts along the central Texas coast
resulting in new attempts to organize and synthesize regional archeological data. Following
Campbell’s original framework, Stephen L. Black (1989) elaborated the taxonomic cultural
sequence of the central Texas coast by defining a series of phases to characterize shorter temporal
intervals focused on the latter half of the region’s prehistory. Recent work by Richard Weinstein
(1992) and Robert A. Ricklis (2004, 2007, 2011) seek to further expand and elaborate the
prehistoric cultural chronology of the central Texas coast. A generalized cultural sequence of the
region is outlined in Table 2.

Paleoindian Period (ca. 10,000–6500 B.C.)
The Texas coastal region underwent dramatic changes in geomorphology throughout the late
Pleistocene and early Holocene. By the peak of glaciation within the Pleistocene (ca. 20,000
B.C.), sea levels were at least 100 m (328 ft) below current levels. Global warming trends ca.
18,000 years ago triggered sea level increases, which by 9,000 years ago allowed for the initial
formation of the modern bay systems of the central Texas coast (Brown et al. 1976; McGowan et
al. 1976). Evidence suggests human migration into the region by approximately 10,000 B.C.,
although the exact timing and nature of the arrival is the subject of some dispute. Due to the
dynamic nature of the Texas coast during the early Holocene, evidence of Paleoindian archeology
is sparse on the central Texas coast and primarily consists of isolated, scattered finds.

18

Despite the lack of good data relating to the Paleoindian period along the central Texas coast,
some attempts have been made to generalize settlement mobility and intensity of site occupation,
drawing on the limited data that are known and on assumptions based on comparisons with other
areas. The people of this period are often characterized as living in small bands of highly mobile
hunter-gatherers, specializing in the hunting of now-extinct Pleistocene megafauna using
thrusting spears with distinctive fluted Clovis-style projectile points. A number of researchers
have seen evidence for a high degree of group mobility in the broad distribution of Paleoindian
artifacts over the landscape and in the variety of presumably nonlocal lithic raw materials from
which they were made (Meltzer and Smith 1986; Shafer 1977; Story 1990:177). Likewise, the
well-documented exploitation of large megafauna by Paleoindians in the western United States
has resulted in the popular conclusion that “big game hunting” was the hallmark of the
Paleoindian subsistence strategy. Increasing evidence suggests that Paleoindian groups were less
dependent upon big game hunting than has been assumed in the past. A more accurate view of
Paleoindian life likely included use of a much wider array of resources (Ferring 1989). Later in
the Early Paleoindian period, Folsom culture subsistence strategies appear to have focused on
hunting bison (Bison antiquus), which would have required a high degree of mobility
accompanied by low population densities (Amick 1996). These populations, however, likely
supplemented their diet with other sources of game (e.g., deer and pronghorn), as well as plant
species (Peter and Weston 1993).
The late Paleoindian period generally appears to be distinguished by the divergence of the earlier,
widespread fluted point tradition into several distinct subtraditions. Like earlier periods of the
Paleoindian stage, the Late Paleoindian period continued to rely on large game. Extinction (or
evolution) of Bison antiquus, however, leads to the focus on modern bison (Bison bison). Some
Late Paleoindian sites also show evidence of use of other animal species. At the Hinds Cave site
in Val Verde County, Texas, the largest proportion (42 percent) of faunal material came from
deer (Lord 1984). A significant amount of smaller vertebrate material was discovered at the site
as well (Lord 1984; Shafer and Bryant 1977). This subsistence strategy is supported by similar
evidence at the Horn Shelter No. 2 site near the Brazos River in Bosque County, Texas, where
skeletal remains from many small animals (e.g., bird, reptile, and small mammal) were found in
association with bison bones (Forrester 1985). In the vicinity of the project area, the Berger Bluff
site (41GD30) appears to display this expanded resource exploitation strategy (Hester et al. 1989;
Weinstein 1992), where bones from small animals such as rabbit, gopher, turtle, fish, and bird
were found in association with Paleoindian living surfaces (Brown 2006).
Late Paleoindian period sites in the region have yielded a variety of point types. Absent are the
fluted point types of the Clovis and Folsom periods, having been replaced by unfluted, lanceolate
projectile points. The Buckner Ranch site (41BE2), for example, produced Plainview,
Golondrina, Angostura, and Scottsbluff points. Meserve points were recovered with Plainview
and Angostura examples at the Morhiss Mound site (41VT1) (Campbell 1976). At the Miller site
(41VT5), Gower and Bell points were recovered in conjunction with other Late Paleoindian types
(Hester 1980). A comprehensive inventory of artifacts found at the Buckeye Knoll site
(41VT98), in the immediate vicinity of the project areas, has not been published, but preliminary
reports indicate the presence of Angostura among other Late Paleoindian point types (Ricklis and
Doran 2003). Typically, the Late Paleoindian period is associated with increased regionalization;
however, Weinstein (1992) also suggests that point type variation seen at some coastal Texas
region sites may reflect chronological differences. However, the Johnson and J-2 Ranch phases
defined within the Late Paleoindian subperiod (Weinstein 1992:59–60) are tentative constructs
based on evidence from a single site each and therefore require supporting evidence from
additional sites before these proposed phases provide interpretive utility.
19

Archaic Period (ca. 6500 B.C.–A.D. 1000)
With the end of Pleistocene glaciation, the prehistoric residents of the region began to develop
into populations of efficient hunter-gatherers exploiting localized resource bases. This period,
and the subsistence pattern that characterizes it, has become termed the “Archaic” period.
Representing a significant period of time (over 7,000 years), this period is characterized by
gradual changes in subsistence patterns, lithic technology, and projectile point styles. The
Archaic period marks the beginning of a more sedentary lifestyle on the central Texas coast.
Archaic populations are usually portrayed as generalized hunters and gatherers with more limited
geographic ranges than the preceding Paleoindian peoples. Local resource use became more
intensive and the material culture displayed much greater diversity, as marked by the appearance
of grinding tools, roasting ovens, rock-lined hearths, and a variety of notched and stemmed
projectile points (Collins 2004). As bison became less available, other resources, including plants
and smaller animal species, were exploited more intensively. Broadly speaking, the archeological
sites dated to this period are larger and more numerous, suggesting a growing human population.
As is true for many areas, a threefold division of the Archaic period, consisting of early, middle,
and late subperiods, has been applied in the region. Dates regarding the Archaic stage
subdivisions vary by area and author (e.g., Black 1989; Collins 2004; Hester 2004; Ricklis 2004;
Ricklis and Doran 2003; Weinstein 1992), but the changes between subperiods are generally
marked by differences in subsistence strategy and projectile point typology (Hofman 1989).

Early Archaic (ca. 6500–4000 B.C.)
The coastal Texas region experienced a rise in sea level as the climate began its transition from
the anathermal to the warmer and wetter altithermal (Frazier 1974). This may have led to
increased shellfish exploitation, particularly oysters (Crassostrea virginica), in substitute of large
game (Ricklis 2004). Within the central Texas coast, Early Archaic projectile points such as
Gower, Wells, Bell, Andice, and Uvalde have been found (Gadus et al. 1993; Weinstein 1992).
Unfortunately, these finds have been in mostly surficial contexts and good dates are not available
for this period in the region (Fox and Hester 1976; Gadus et al. 1993; Weinstein 2002).
Consequently, the dates provided for this region’s Early Archaic period are based on similarities
in point morphologies with dated materials in Central Texas (Weinstein 1992, 2002).

Middle Archaic (ca. 4000–2000 B.C.)
The Middle Archaic brought sea level stabilization and increased exploitation of coastal resources
in the region (Ricklis and Blum 1997) with the extension of the San Antonio estuary system
farther north than in previous periods (Prewitt and Paine 1988; Weinstein 1992). Sites with
Middle Archaic components are slightly more numerous than earlier periods. As with the Early
Archaic, many Middle Archaic sites in the region lack secure radiocarbon dates (Gadus et al.
1993). Consequently, temporal estimates for this period are inferred based on point type
chronologies developed in Central Texas (Hester 1980). Generally, the Middle Archaic across
the Texas coast is marked by the proliferation of triangular dart points (Hester 1980). Middle
Archaic projectile point types found in the area include Bulverde, Nolan, Travis, Palmillas,
Pedernales, Kinney, and Tortugas styles (Weinstein 2002). At the nearby Buckeye Knoll site,
early triangular and Refugio dart points were discovered with evidence of short-term occupation
(Ricklis and Doran 2003). Both the Blue Bayou (41VT94) and Sentinel Oak (41VT79) sites have
20

been tentatively associated with the Middle Archaic subperiod (Weinstein 1992). Dated oyster
reef deposits collected 4.8 km (3 mi) north of Green Lake indicate that by the end of the Middle
Archaic, productive estuary resource systems were well established for the lower Guadalupe
River valley (Weinstein 1992). With the gradual stabilization of sea levels and establishment of
active estuary systems throughout the Middle Archaic, the central Texas coast likely became a
more predictable environment for the procurement of marine and brackish-water fish and
mollusks. Throughout this period, Pleistocene terraces were the preferred locations for habitation
sites.

Late Archaic (ca. 2000 B.C.–A.D. 1000)
In the early part of the Late Archaic subperiod, a rapid rise in sea level took place, but returned to
a stable, modern level by the middle Late Archaic (Prewitt and Paine 1988; Ricklis and Blum
1997). The number of archeological sites attributed to the Late Archaic period on the central
Texas coast is substantially greater than for previous periods. The number of sites in estuarine
habitats increases dramatically during the Late Archaic. It has been suggested the combination of
sea level stabilization, increase in river sediments, and the gradual formation of Matagorda Island
would have acted to reduce salinity of the San Antonio Bay system, thereby increasing the
productivity of oysters and other estuarine resources (Nelson and Bray 1970; Pearson et al. 1986)
and leading to an increase in local populations. Concomitantly, evidence of Late Archaic
subsistence is dominated by fish species—black drum, redfish, spotted sea trout, and Atlantic
croaker—whose annual cycle is tied to estuary bay systems (Ricklis 2004). Although fishing
appears to be a primary subsistence strategy during this period along coastal Texas, the presence
of projectile points and mammal faunal material indicates that hunting was not abandoned in the
Late Archaic (Ricklis and Blum 1997). Late Archaic projectile point types common to the area
include Marshall, Williams, Castroville, Ensor, Darl, and Fairland dart points (Weinstein 2002).
From Morhiss Mound, Late Archaic diagnostic material includes Morhiss, Marshall, Lange,
Marcos, Darl, Ensor, and Fairland projectile points (Campbell 1976; Suhm and Krieger 1954) and
Campbell (1976) suggests that the majority of the burials from Morhiss Mound were interred
during the Late Archaic period. Pedernales, Morhiss, Lange, Fairland, and Ensor style projectile
points were among the artifacts recovered from the nearby Buckeye Knoll site. In addition, the
site produced several hearths and a formal cemetery dating to the Late Archaic (Ricklis 2011;
Ricklis and Doran 2003).
In addition to the increased reliance upon estuarine resources, the archeology of the Late Archaic
reveals a diverse shell tool industry and evidence of basketry (Campbell 1947, 1952; Ricklis
1990, 2004). Although perforated oyster shell and edge-flaked clamshell scrapers are known
from the Early Archaic and continue to be found in later periods, the use of conch shell for tools
appears to begin only in the Late Archaic (Campbell 1952; Ricklis 1990, 2004). Additionally, the
number of bone artifacts (e.g., awls, socketed points) increases dramatically in the archeological
record of the Late Archaic. As the central Texas coast estuary bay systems began to increase its
biotic productivity throughout the Late Archaic, areas such as the lower Guadalupe River valley
possibly began to offer more opportunities for prehistoric activities. In addition to the continued
use of existing site locations (e.g., Morhiss Mound and Buckeye Knoll) in the region during the
Late Archaic, other areas (both upriver and downriver) were for the first time associated with
significant increases in prehistoric populations. The increase in both the number of sites and use
of estuarine resources is a trend that continues into the Late Prehistoric period.
21

Late Prehistoric (ca. A.D. 1000–1700)
Using a socio-cultural evolutionary taxonomic system, the end of the Late Archaic period on the
central Texas coast, like the remainder of the state, is marked by the widespread adoption of the
bow and arrow and ceramics (Hester 1975, 1981; Suhm and Krieger 1954). Based on changes in
artifact types and suspected shifts in subsistence practices, the Late Prehistoric period for the
central Texas coast has been divided into various phases by several authors. Weinstein (1992:63–
65) subdivided the period between A.D. 700 and A.D. 1700 into a Late Prehistoric period and a
tentative Protohistoric period, with the former period further divided into two phases (Blue Bayou
[A.D. 700–950] and Anaqua [A.D. 950–1150]) and the later (A.D. 1150–1700) divided into an
inland (Berclair phase) and a coastal (Rockport phase) geographic expressions. Ricklis (2004),
conversely, has proposed only two subperiods spanning the Late Prehistoric interval: the Initial
Late Prehistoric (ca. A.D. 1000–1250/1300) and the Final Late Prehistoric (ca. A.D. 1250/1300–
1700).
The Initial Late Prehistoric phase along the central Texas coast is characterized by Scallorn and
Fresno arrow points along with plain, sandy paste ceramics similar to Goose Creek of the upper
Texas coast (Ricklis 2004; Weinstein 1992). By the Final Late Prehistoric phase, Perdiz arrow
points predominate along with a prismatic blade technology, thin bifacial knives, and Rockport
sandy paste ceramics (Fritz 1975; Ricklis 1996). Rockport ceramics are quite distinctive because
many have asphaltum decorations and were produced in a variety of shapes (e.g., bowls, jars, and
ollas) (Ricklis 1996).
Fishing continued to be a major focus of subsistence in the Late Prehistoric period and was
possibly of even greater importance than in the Late Archaic (Ricklis 1990, 1996; Weinstein
1992). At several sites (e.g., 41CL2) along the central Texas coast, Late Prehistoric fish-rich
middens are stratigraphically above Late Archaic shell middens, suggesting a possible shift in
resource selection (Weinstein 1992). Additionally, by A.D. 1250/1300, bison became a significant
resource for the region as numerous upland hunting camps, such as 41RF21, have been dated to
this period (Ricklis 1989, 1990, 1996). The abundance of subsistence data gathered from Late
Prehistoric sites has made it possible to address issues of seasonality and group migration.
Analysis of fish otolith data from large shoreline sites has shown that the most important species
(black drum and redfish) were collected during the fall through early spring periods (Ricklis
1988, 1990, 1996). Additional data from Rangia cuneata shells in river margin sites have
revealed late spring through summer occupations (Aten 1981; Carlson 1988; Ricklis 2004).
Collectively, these data along with site size information suggest large aggregations of groups
occurred during the fall and winter months along the coast, followed by a late spring and summer
dispersal of smaller groups into upland river margin settings. Ricklis (1990, 1992, 1996), using
archival sources, has suggested similar seasonality patterns existed within the early historic
period Karankawa along the central Texas coast.
Late Prehistoric sites occupy a range of settings from the upper reaches of San Antonio Bay to
just north of Victoria along the Guadalupe River. Some of the major sites from earlier periods
(e.g., Morhiss Mound, Sentinel Oak, Buckeye Knoll) have very small Late Prehistoric
components, but others (e.g., the Linn Lake [41VT81] and Blue Bayou sites) either continued or
took on greater significance during the Late Prehistoric period (Weinstein 1992). The larger sites
for this period appear to be near the coast and appear to have operated as large aggregation
centers for winter fishing. One such aggregation center is the Guadalupe Bay (41CL2) site.
Although first established in the Late Archaic, the Guadalupe Bay site has a significant Late
22

Prehistoric component composed of Rockport ceramics, Fresno arrow points, and large amounts
of faunal material. Smaller sites for this period are those located upstream along upland settings,
often at the river margin. Many of the smaller upland sites are indicative of hunting camps such
as the Mellon site (41RF21), which yielded concentrations of deer and bison bone (Ricklis 2004).
Settlement patterns appear to have changed by the Late Prehistoric, whereby the larger sites
upstream along the river margin such as Buckeye Knoll and Morhiss Mound were largely
abandoned, as large aggregation sites along the coastal edge, such as Guadalupe Bay, became
prevalent. At the same time that upland sites such as Morhiss Mound were falling out of use,
many more were being created upstream along the river margin as short-term hunting/fishing
camps. In some cases, sites that were active in earlier periods such as Linn Lake appear to be the
focus of hunting and possible trade activities well into the historic period (Hester 1985; Weinstein
1992).

Early Historic Period (ca. A.D. 1700–1800)
The current review is limited to the earliest component of the established historic record and
focuses solely on sites with primarily indigenous components; however, few sites in the region
meet these criteria. Subsistence and seasonality data from the early historic period are severely
lacking and currently restrict our understanding of indigenous adaptation for the central Texas
coast during the first few centuries of European interaction. Multicomponent sites Blue Bayou
and Linn Lake both contain early European artifacts; additionally, a glass Guerrero-style
projectile point was found at Linn Lake (Hester 1985). Downstream in the Green Lake vicinity,
Spanish artifacts have been recovered at several sites (41CL77, 41CL7, and 41CL2) that are
believed to have been associated with the late eighteenth-century Mission Nuestra Señora de
Refugio or one of its associated ranches. Although the original location of this mission is not
known, it is has been suggested that site 41CL10 may in fact represent its eighteenth-century
location (Henson 1992).

HISTORIC CONTEXT

European Exploration and Early Settlement
The first European explorers reached the vicinity now encompassed by Victoria County during
the early sixteenth century. In 1528, Álvar Núñez Cabeza de Vaca, traversed the area but made
no attempt at permanent settlement. More than a century later, in 1685, René Robert Cavelier,
Sieur de La Salle, established Fort St. Louis, on behalf of France, on the west bank of Garcitas
Creek. The Spanish government soon took exception to French encroachments in the Gulf Coast
area. On 14 April 1689, Alonso de León discovered the Guadalupe River while on a mission
from the Spanish government to find and destroy Fort St. Louis. Father Damián Massanet, who
accompanied the expedition, named the river in honor of the patron saint of Mexico. De León
ultimately discovered that Fort St. Louis had been abandoned, with its colonists fallen victim
either to disease or to armed engagements with local Native American tribes (Morris 1953:n.p.;
Roell 1990:126).

23

In 1722, Presidio Nuestra Señora de Loreto, built on the ruins of Fort St. Louis at Garcitas Creek,
marked the first Spanish settlement in what became Victoria County. On the east bank of the
creek in what is now Jackson County, Mission Nuestra Señora del Espíritu Santo de Zúñiga (La
Bahía) was established (Morris 1953:n.p.), but both the mission and presidio were moved to the
Guadalupe River in August 1726. The Spanish built a dam across the river and added an
irrigation system to supply nearby agricultural fields. The area around the mission became
known as Mission Valley, an appellation it has retained (Morris 1953:n.p.). The mission and
presidio moved again in 1749, this time to the San Antonio River in present-day Goliad County.
Despite the relocations, the settlers enjoyed stability and prosperity by cultivating crops and
producing enough hay and corn to export their surplus to San Antonio and settlements in East
Texas. The mission also raised livestock, and an 1858 inventory listed 3,220 branded cattle, 120
horses, and 1,600 sheep (Roell 1990:126).
After La Bahía moved to the San Antonio River in 1749, no further permanent settlement
occurred in Victoria County other than isolated individual holdings, such as Carlos de la Garza’s
rancho at Anaqua and Margaret Wright’s homestead at Mission Valley (Roell 1990:126). In
1821, Mexico won its independence from Spain. The newly formed Mexican government
retained control over the vast holdings in what is now the American Southwest, including Texas.
Three years later, Don Martín de León successfully petitioned the Mexican government to
establish a colony on the Texas gulf coastal plain. The colony encompassed all of the territory of
present-day Victoria and Calhoun counties, as well as parts of Lavaca, Jackson, and DeWitt
counties. The center of government for the colony was the Nuestra Señora de Guadalupe de
Jesús Victoria settlement. Soon known as Guadalupe Victoria, the community prospered, with a
population of approximately 300 in 1834.
In 1824, the government of Mexico approved a charter petitioned by Don Martín de León to
establish a colony of 41 families between the Guadalupe and Lavaca rivers. The De León Colony
settled in what is now present day Victoria. Each colonist received a town lot, one league (4,228
acres) and one labor (177 acres; Roell 2012). Early General Land Office (GLO) maps of presentday Victoria County show land grants patented in the 1830s to numerous individuals with
Spanish surnames. In 1833, Polito Castillo, a colonist of the De León Colony, was granted 4,428
acres of land on the west bank of the Guadalupe River approximately 7.5 miles south of Victoria,
which coincides with the proposed Industrial Park South project area (GLO 2012). In that same
year, Polito Castillo sold the entire league of land to Philip Dimitt, who owned the land until his
death in ca. 1845.
The Texas Revolution began in 1836, and the nearby towns of Victoria and Goliad became
crucial points in the Revolution. Goliad was the later name of the former Spanish presidio La
Bahía, and it stood 26 miles west of the town of Victoria. Representing the northeastern point of
defense for the Texas revolution, James W. Fannin, Jr., commanded 500 soldiers at Goliad.
While determining what move to make in support of the revolution in early 1836, Fannin received
orders from Maj. Gen. Sam Houston to march to San Antonio to assist the Texan soldiers at the
Alamo. Houston soon rescinded the orders, however, and commanded Fannin to meet with other
troops in Victoria. Fannin, concerned that Goliad might be attacked, did not leave immediately.
When he finally headed his troops toward Victoria, they soon encountered Gen. José de Urrea of
the Mexican Army. Outnumbered, Fannin surrendered, believing that his troops would be treated
properly as prisoners of war. He and his troops were marched back to Goliad, however,
whereupon Mexican President Santa Anna ordered their execution.
24

After the declaration of Texas independence, Victoria County stood among the original 23
counties established by the First Congress of the Republic of Texas in March 1836, and three
years later, the city of Victoria became the third city to receive a formal charter by the republic.
John J. Linn, an Irish immigrant and member of one of the original 41 families of Guadalupe
Victoria, served as a delegate to the convention in which Texas declared its independence and
established the Republic of Texas. Linn not only served in the republic’s congress and helped to
draft the new nation’s constitution, but also was Victoria’s first mayor (Hammonds 1999:11;
Roell 1990:127; Shook 1990:113). Although the de Leóns and other Hispanic settlers in
Guadalupe Victoria supported the Texans in their bid for independence, most were ostracized
after the war and forced to flee. Anglo-Americans eventually resettled the area (Hammonds
1999:9, 11–12; Roell 1990:127; Shook 1990:113).
After Texas voters approved an annexation ordinance and state constitution, Texas joined the
United States in 1845, with formal transfer of government taking place the following February.
The same year, the Texas legislature settled conflicting territorial claims among Victoria, La
Vaca, Jackson, and Calhoun counties, and Victoria County’s boundaries were finalized to its
present configuration (Roell 1990:127).

Nineteenth-century Development
Victoria County was a crossroads for southern Texas long before statehood had been established.
A cart road extended from the port at Indianola to San Antonio and New Braunfels, and the old
Goliad Road traversed from east to west. Located alongside the Guadalupe River, the city of
Victoria strove to secure its position as a trade center. In 1840, the county commissioners
approved rates for a municipal ferry across the river. A toll bridge, built by Richard Owens and
Sylvester Sutton, followed in 1851 (Roell 1990:127). Both Kemper’s Bluff and White’s Ferry,
also in Victoria County, posed the most serious competition to Victoria as trade centers and
embarkation points (Roell 1990:127). Situated along the Guadalupe River, Kemper’s Bluff stood
north of the present site of the McFaddin community, while nearby White’s Ferry was a short
distance southeast and downriver at the confluence of the Guadalupe and Old rivers.
The competition among the river towns reflected the Guadalupe River’s early supremacy in
creating trade routes. The 255-mile-long river rises in Kerr County and flows into San Antonio
Bay, and its tributaries include the Blanco, Comal, and San Marcos rivers. The Guadalupe joins
the San Antonio River at the southeast corner of Victoria County (Morris 1953:n.p.). The
legislature of Coahuila and Texas attempted to improve the river’s navigability in 1828, 1833,
and 1835, but the Texas revolution foiled these efforts. The Republic of Texas authorized
improvements in 1840, and Victoria devoted one-fourth of the proceeds from the sale of its public
lands for this purpose. The Texas state legislature authorized further work in 1853 (Morris
1953:n.p.; Roell 1990:127).
Construction of the San Antonio & Mexican Gulf Railroad from Victoria to Port Lavaca in 1861
marked the decline of river dominance in transportation. During the Civil War, Confederate
General John B. Magruder rendered the Guadalupe River impassable by sinking boats and trees
to prevent Federal troops from using the waterway. Magruder ordered destruction of the railroad
tracks as well, but these were rebuilt immediately after the war. The Guadalupe River, on the
other hand, fell out of use until the 1880s. Storm and flood damage to the port town of Indianola
in 1875 and 1886 further hampered efforts to resume river commerce (Morris 1953:n.p.; Roell
1990:128).
25

Victoria County voted for secession in 1861 and approximately 300 male residents served with
the Confederate Army. Camp Henry E. McCulloch was established about 4 miles from Victoria
under the command of Colonel R. R. Garland. The county also functioned as a transportation,
military, and supply center through the early years of the Civil War. The city of Victoria stood on
the Cotton Road, which provided access to guns, ammunition, medicine, and supplies from
Mexico in exchange for crops. Confederate troops withdrew from the area following Magruder’s
order in 1863 to destroy navigation on the Guadalupe River and the tracks of the San Antonio &
Mexican Gulf railroad (Hammonds 1999:14–15; Victoria Sesquicentennial, Inc., 1974:51).
Major General David S. Stanley arrived in July 1865 with Federal troops to occupy the city, thus
beginning the Reconstruction period. In addition to ruined transportation routes, the county
suffered extensive economic losses as a result of the Civil War, with the value of all property
declining from $3,088,476 in 1860 to $1,503,295 in 1870. The 1860 property enumeration
included considerable value that was embodied by the county’s 1,413 slaves, but the 1870s
census did not include slaves as property (Hammonds 1999:14–15; Roell 1990:128).
The agricultural economy that had depended on slave labor for much of its profitability had to be
reconstituted. For the short term, labor-intensive cotton was abandoned in favor of raising cattle
and other livestock. Production figures for 1870 showed that only 205 bales of cotton were
produced, while the number of cattle grew to 61,651 (Roell 1990:129). A growing manufacturing
base also developed, with the number of manufacturing establishments increasing from 10 to 25
by 1870. Reflective of the local agricultural economy, one of the area’s first meat-packing plants
was established in 1869. An ample labor supply could be drawn from freed African Americans,
migrants from other parts of the United States, and European immigrants. Since the 1840s,
German immigrants had been arriving in Victoria County, many of them lured by the German
Immigration Society and seeking relief from the revolutions and oppression of their native
country. Italians provided a substantial portion of the labor for railroad construction and often
settled permanently in Victoria (Hammonds 1999:11; Roell 1990:127, 129; Shook 1990:115).
Victoria’s strategic geographic location and transportation routes provided the basis for the
county’s economic resurgence after Reconstruction ended. The city’s bridge across the
Guadalupe River was rebuilt as early as 1865 by Jessie Obadiah Wheeler. By 1873, the Gulf,
Western Texas & Pacific Railroad connected Victoria with Cuero and the coast, and in 1882, the
New York, Texas & Mexican Railroad provided the first cross-country route. These lines, as well
as the old Goliad and Indianola roads and the city’s status as county seat, made Victoria the
logical focal point for shipping and commercial activity for both Victoria and surrounding
counties (Hammonds 1999:15; Roell 1990:129). Improved transportation also spurred more
development in the city. For example, in 1882, A. de Rollepot established an ice manufactory in
Victoria. Local ranchers seized on the opportunity, and the following year, James McFaddin,
along with Thomas M. O’Connor, G. A. Levi, Leo N. Levi, J. M. Mathis, Tobe Wood, and others,
founded the Texas Continental Meat Company. With a capital of $1 million, the joint stock
company’s mission was to use the manufactured ice to run refrigerated train cars filled with fresh
meat to eastern cities (Petty 1961:75–76). The history of cattle ranching in the region earned
Victoria County the nickname “Cradle of the Texas Cattle Industry” (Hammonds 1999:19).

CONCLUSIONS
Although the body of knowledge regarding central Texas coast archeology has grown greatly in
recent decades, it is still poorly understood overall. The prehistory of the region was intricately
tied to its slowly evolving estuary systems and resulting changes in biotic productivity. As sea
26

levels began to reach modern levels ca. 3,000 years ago, prehistoric settlement along the central
Texas coast began to increase. During the Paleoindian and Early–Middle Archaic periods, sites
predominate along river margin settings. Middle Archaic–Early Historic period sites, such as
Linn Lake, attest to the long-term suitability of this area for prehistoric habitation, with increased
use of bay/shoreline localities as estuarine productivity developed. Historically, settlement along
the central Texas coast was intrinsically tied to water courses such as the Guadalupe River, much
like the preceding prehistoric periods. Early mission settlements and later ranches and
homesteaders were closely tied to the Guadalupe River and its tributary systems. Postbellum
settlement of the region continued to focus on waterways, and the rapidly developing road
system.

27

CHAPTER 4
METHODOLOGY

The cultural resources survey recorded in this document was designed to identify both prehistoric
and historic archeological sites and important architectural resources located within the proposed
project areas. This work was undertaken with two primary management goals:
1. To locate cultural resources occurring within the designated survey areas
2. To assess the significance of each resource in regard to its potential for inclusion in the
National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) or designation as a State Archeological
Landmark (SAL)
The first of these goals was accomplished by a program of background and archival research,
intensive pedestrian survey, and excavations undertaken using the field methods described in this
chapter. The second goal consists of assessment and evaluation based on National Register of
Historic Places criteria defined in 36 Code of Federal Regulations (CFR) §60.4 (National Park
Service 1990) and the Antiquities Code of Texas.

PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS
Although some important archeological sites in the Guadalupe River valley were initially
documented in the early 1880s (Morhiss Mound [41VT1] and the Peach Knoll [41VT89] site
[Rose 1883:9]), professional archeological investigations in the region were not initiated until the
early 1930s. A. M. Woolsey surveyed sections of the Guadalupe River valley in 1932 (Campbell
1976; Weinstein 1992). Woolsey’s work included surveys of the lower Guadalupe River from
San Antonio Bay to Green Lake, recording and testing a series of shell midden sites (Weinstein
1992). At that time, Woolsey also surveyed and tested the Morhiss Mound site along the
Guadalupe River, just north of Coleto Creek (Campbell 1976). Further excavation of Morhiss
Mound was performed as part of a 16-month Works Progress Administration (WPA) project that
began in 1938 (Duffen 1940). During the later portion of the twentieth century, the activities of
local vocational archeologists, university graduate students and research staff, and professional
cultural resources management firms have contributed to documenting archeological sites and
synthesizing the history and prehistory of the region.

29

To determine the extent and intensity of previous cultural resources survey efforts as well as the
locations of previously recorded historic or archeological resources in the vicinity of the project
areas, the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas online database maintained by the Texas Historical
Commission (THC) was consulted, as well as background literature and cultural resources
management reports from the region. Review of these records indicated that eight previous
cultural resources surveys have been conducted within a 1.6-km (1-mi) radius of the proposed
project area (Figure 7).
In the early 1980s, prior to the widespread implementation of modern cultural resources
management surveys along the Victoria Barge Canal, two prehistoric archeological sites, Sentinel
Oak (41VT79) and Blue Bayou (41VT94), were documented by local vocational archeologists.
Reports of the excavation and bioarcheological analysis of human skeletal remains from the Blue
Bayou site were published as Master’s theses (Comuzzie 1987; Huebner 1988). A detailed
summary of the excavation and biological analysis of the Blue Bayou (41VT94) site is provided
by Weinstein (1992:52–54). In 1982, as part of an archeological reconnaissance survey for the
U.S. Army Corps of Engineers, Galveston District, Carolyn Murphy recorded a series of sites
along the Victoria Barge Canal, including sites 41VT98 and 41VT99 in the vicinity of the project
area (Weinstein 1992). Coastal Environments, Inc., began intensive archeological survey along
approximately 16 km of the Victoria Barge Canal in 1998, recording six new archeological sites,
including sites 41VT101, 41VT102, and 41VT103 in the vicinity of the current project areas, and
evaluating seven sites adjacent to the canal, including nearby sites 41VT98 and 41VT99
(Weinstein 1992). Site 41VT98 was revisited in 1991 to assess the impacts of a pipeline
relocation project and to make recommendations regarding which portions of the site should be
avoided. Fifteen auger tests and one test unit were excavated in the southwestern portion of the
site, confirming the significance of the archeological deposits preserved in the central site area
(Weinstein 1991:28). Cultural resources survey and evaluation for proposed dredge disposal
areas along the barge canal was performed by Prewitt and Associates, Inc., in 1993 and resulted
in the recording of site 41VT113 (the Dalton Bridge site) and revisiting nine previously recorded
sites (including 41VT98, 41VT99, and 41VT103) to determine their condition and make
recommendations regarding eligibility for inclusion in the National Register (Gadus et al. 1993).
More recent work by Prewitt and Associates was conducted between 1995 and 1998 as part of the
proposed widening of the Victoria Barge Canal (Gadus et al. 1999). Test excavations were
carried out at six sites, including 41VT103, which was recommended not eligible for inclusion in
the NRHP (Gadus et al. 1999). Site 41VT98 underwent continued testing/mitigation excavation
by Coastal Environments in 2000. Directed by Robert Ricklis, the continued work at 41VT98
involved the excavation of a large prehistoric cemetery (approximately 75 individuals) in addition
to extensive cultural deposits dating from the Early Archaic to the Late Prehistoric periods
(Ricklis 2011). A detailed published report of the 41VT98 excavations is forthcoming (Ricklis
2007, 2011). In January and February 2008, Geo-Marine, Inc., conducted a cultural resources
survey for the Port of Victoria’s proposed barge mooring facility (Meyer 2008). This survey
encompassed an area of approximately 193 acres of the Guadalupe River floodplain immediately
adjacent to the current proposed Industrial Park South project area (see Figure 7). With the
exception of two historic period isolated localities, no resources were encountered during the
survey (Meyer 2008:47 48). As a result of these previous surveys, eight archeological sites have
been previously documented within a 1-km (0.6-mi) radius of the proposed project areas (Table
3).
