Based on the sampling strategies and geoarcheological investigations utilized during the nearby
Victoria County Station cultural resources survey (Peter and Prior 2008; Tinsley 2010), it is clear
that archeological site potential is highly variable across the various landforms that occur within
the lower Guadalupe River valley. Cross-referencing the locations of nearby previously recorded
prehistoric archeological sites with geomorphic features such as landforms and soils suggests that
prehistoric occupation of the area was typically confined to upland escarpments and terraces
along valley margins and to prominent point bar and natural levee landforms near the Guadalupe
River. Based on the documentation and geomorphic position of previously recorded prehistoric
archeological sites in the vicinity of the proposed project areas, portions of both project areas
were determined to have a high potential of containing prehistoric archeological sites. Although
the Pickering Basin project area has been adversely impacted by industrial development, the soils
in the proposed project area are likely of sufficient depth to contain prehistoric archeological
deposits. In the proposed Industrial Park South project area, the floodplain of the Guadalupe
River has the potential to preserve deeply buried prehistoric archeological deposits. In addition,
there is a high potential for prehistoric and historic period archeological sites to occur on
landforms along the valley margin and escarpment. Conversely, the relatively level, featureless,
and poorly drained uplands hold a low potential for prehistoric archeological sites to occur;
however, historic period sites may occur in areas of historic development and along transportation
corridors, such as the Old Bloomington Road. Various sources of historical and geographic data
were consulted to ascertain the likelihood of historic-era structural resources existing within the
proposed project area. These sources include historic highway maps, topographic maps, and soil
surveys (which often record cultural resources such as cemeteries, houses, and related features),
and aerial photography. Based on a review of these historic records, it is evident that the region
in general has experienced a dynamic history. Evaluating these sources with more recent records,
however, indicates that the substantial development that has occurred in recent years has altered
much of the historic landscape. Historic period habitation within the proposed project areas was
minimal during the nineteenth century. Settlement increased markedly during the twentieth
century, primarily along Old Bloomington Road.

32

ARCHEOLOGICAL SURVEY METHODS
Due to the variability in prehistoric and historic habitation and archeological site preservation
across the landforms found in the proposed project areas, several survey and sampling methods
were used during this investigation. These investigations included the use of backhoe trenching
and systematic shovel testing at varying intervals to locate and define cultural deposits.
In the Pickering Basin project area and the floodplain portion of the Industrial Park South project
area, systematic backhoe trenching was the primary survey method used to locate archeological
deposits deeply buried by Holocene alluvium. Backhoe trenches were approximately 5–7 m (16–
23 ft) in length and were excavated to a depth of 4 m (13 ft) where possible. Samples of the
trench fill from each natural strata were screened or otherwise inspected for artifacts, and a
portion of the exposed soil profile was inspected, recorded by natural strata, and photographed.
Along the Guadalupe valley margin and within the Pleistocene uplands, systematic shovel tests
were the primary method used to locate archeological deposits. Shovel tests consisted of handexcavated units 40 centimeters (cm; 15.7 inches [in]) in diameter excavated in 20-cm (7.9-in)
levels, with the excavated soil passed through a 0.64-cm (0.25-in) hardware mesh screen to
recover artifacts. Shovel tests were excavated to the Bt horizon, typically to depths ranging
between 20 and 130 cm (8–51 in), based on varying soil conditions encountered. The location,
results, and exposed soil profile of selected shovel tests were recorded with a submeter-accurate
Trimble® GeoXH™ global positioning system (GPS) unit. Shovel tests not recorded via GPS
were plotted on field maps and manually entered into the project’s geographic information system
(GIS).
Along the Guadalupe valley margin, shovel tests were excavated in 100-m (328-ft) intervals, with
additional shovel tests placed judgmentally in areas deemed to have a high probability of
preserving archeological deposits and in areas where artifacts were exposed at the surface.
Shovel tests excavated across the Pleistocene uplands were placed in 3-acre quadrats, at the
locations of map-documented historic period structures, and where surface artifacts were
encountered.

ARCHEOLOGICAL SITE DEFINITION
An archeological site is typically considered to be a spatially discrete area containing cultural
resources. The recognition of a “site” is therefore contingent on the basis of content and extent.
Content refers to artifacts recovered from surface or subsurface contexts, architectural features, or
other manifestations of past human activity. The extent of a site is based on the vertical and
horizontal spatial arrangement of these cultural remains. For surficial materials, a site is defined
as five or more artifacts of at least two different materials or functional classes or at least two
cultural features located within the same vicinity (typically a 400-m2 [0.1-acre] area). The extent
of the surface artifacts and cultural features are then defined as the site boundary. When artifacts
are encountered in buried contexts, a site is defined within the extent of the culturally positive
excavations. In cases where shovel tests yielded cultural materials, additional delineation shovel
tests were excavated to define the boundary of the sites. Shovel tests used to define and delineate
historic period site boundaries were placed at 20-m (66-ft) intervals in a cruciform pattern around
the initial positive shovel test until two negative shovel tests were reached. For prehistoric sites,
delineation shovel tests were excavated in a 20-m-interval (66-ft) grid until two negative shovel
tests were encountered in each grid direction.
33

The spatial extent of a site is defined within the extent of culturally positive shovel tests plus a
20-m (66-ft) buffer. Sites may also be defined within the extent of surface artifacts or features
and positive excavations, within the prescribed buffer, when both are present. Cultural remains
meeting these criteria are designated as a site, recorded on a Texas Archeological Site Data Form,
and submitted to the Texas Archeology Laboratory (TARL) to be included in the Texas
Archeological Sites Atlas database. Depending on depositional integrity and cultural content,
archeological sites can be eligible for inclusion in the NRHP. Conversely, discovery of cultural
materials that do not meet these criteria are considered isolated occurrences of past human
activity and are simply documented by location and content. Modern materials and features (i.e.,
less than 50 years old) are not considered sites, with only location and content documented.
Cultural isolates and modern features are not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP because of their
failure to meet site definition and their inability to contribute important information.

NATIONAL REGISTER OF HISTORIC PLACES ELIGIBILITY CRITERIA
The assessment of significance of a cultural resources property is based on federal guidelines and
regulations. The criteria (36 CFR 60.4 [a–d]) for evaluating properties for inclusion in the NRHP
are codified under the authority of the National Historic Preservation Act of 1966, as amended,
and the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation has set forth guidelines to use in determining
site eligibility. Based Advisory Council guidelines, any resource that is included in or eligible for
inclusion in the National Register is a “historic property.” “The term ‘eligible for inclusion in the
National Register’ includes both properties formally determined as such by the Secretary of the
Interior and all other properties that meet National Register listing criteria” (36 CFR §800.2 [e]).
Subsequent to the identification of relevant historical themes and related research questions, the
following four criteria for eligibility are applied:
The quality of significance in American history, architecture, archeology, engineering, and culture is
present in districts, sites, buildings, structures, and objects that possess integrity of location, design,
setting, material, workmanship, feeling, and association and
(a) that are associated with events that have made a significant contribution to the broad patterns of
our history; or
(b) that are associated with the lives of persons significant in our past; or
(c) that embody the distinctive characteristics of a type, period, or method of construction, or that
represent the work of a master, or that possess high artistic values, or that represent a significant
and distinguishable entity whose components may lack individual distinction; or
(d) that have yielded or may be likely to yield, information important in prehistory or history.
[36 CFR 60.4 (a–d)]

The principal research objective is to determine whether a cultural resources property possesses
the potential to contribute to one or more of the above-defined criteria. Therefore, adequate
information on site function, context, and chronological placement from both archeological and,
if appropriate, historical perspectives is essential for cultural resources evaluations. Because
research questions vary as a result of geography, temporal period, and project design,
determination of site context and chronological placement of cultural properties is a particularly
important objective during the inventory process. The objective of this current project was to
locate and define both horizontally and vertically any cultural resources, document and describe
those resources, and then, when adequate data were present, evaluate each for NRHP eligibility.

34

STATE ARCHEOLOGICAL LANDMARK DESIGNATION
Under the Antiquities Code of Texas, archeological sites may be considered significant at the
state level and be recognized or designated as an SAL, provided that at least one of the following
conditions is met:
(1) The archeological site is situated on lands owned or controlled by the State of Texas or one of its
political subdivisions; or
(2) The archeological site is situated on private lands that have been specifically designated as an
SAL . . . and fits at least one of the following criteria:
(A) Preservation of materials must be sufficient to allow application of standard archeological
techniques to advantage;
(B) The majority of artifacts are in place so that a significant portion of the site’s original
characteristics can be defined through investigation;
(C) The site has the potential to contribute to cumulative culture history by the addition of new
information;
(D) The site offers evidence of unique or rare attributes; and/or
(E) The site offers a unique and rare opportunity to test techniques, theory, or methods or
preservation, thereby contributing to scientific knowledge [Texas Natural Resources Code
1977; Title 9, Chapter 191, Texas Antiquities Committee, Section 191.094 and Chapter
41.7, Antiquities Code of Texas].

If a property is owned by a political subentity of Texas, such as the Port of Victoria, it technically
meets SAL Condition 1. At issue, however, is whether a site meeting that criterion is significant
and eligible for designation as an SAL or for inclusion in the National Register. The principal
research objective at the survey level of investigation is the determination of whether a cultural
resources site possesses the potential to make a contribution to one or more of the aforementioned
criteria. The objective is to determine which cultural resources could yield further data regarding
regional, methodological, or theoretical research questions. Therefore, adequate information on
site function, context, integrity, and chronological placement from both archeological and, if
appropriate, historical perspectives is essential for cultural investigations. Because research
questions vary as a result of geography and temporal period, determination of site context and
chronological placement of cultural resources is a particularly important objective during the
inventory process.

ARTIFACT TREATMENT AND ANALYSIS METHODS
All artifacts recovered in subsurface contexts and temporally or functionally diagnostic artifacts
encountered in surface contexts were briefly examined, bagged, and labeled by provenience in the
field. Upon arrival at the Geo-Marine laboratory facilities in Plano, Texas, artifacts were
inventoried, cleaned, analyzed, and catalogued (Appendix A). Each artifact was thoroughly
examined in order to identify and determine, to the extent possible, its function or role and
cultural or chronological affiliation within the identified contexts.

Prehistoric Artifact Analysis
The majority of the prehistoric artifacts recovered during this project consisted of chipped lithic
artifacts, specifically chipped lithic debitage. The chipped stone artifact category includes all
chipping or flaking debris and intentionally chipped or flaked stone artifacts. Prehistoric chipped
35

stone artifacts, likewise, include several classes of tools representing different uses and functions.
Debitage refers to the waste flakes and angular material (shatter) generated during the
manufacture and maintenance of chipped stone tools. In some cases, these flakes served as
expedient tools for a variety of cutting, scraping, or perforating functions. All lithic artifacts were
classified to the most specific morpho-functional class possible, and counts, weights, and raw
material types were recorded for each artifact or group of similar artifacts from each provenience.
The prehistoric ceramic assemblages recovered by these investigation consists of two categories
of artifacts: fired clay and ceramic vessel fragments. Fired clay is a general category for all
fragments of thermally altered clay that do not exhibit characteristics identifiable to a function or
other ceramic artifact class. The single prehistoric ceramic vessel fragment recovered through
this survey effort was identified based on tempering agent and surface treatment.

Historic Artifact Analysis
Historic period artifacts recovered during this survey were examined by laboratory staff for
particular attributes and diagnostic traits using morphological and/or functional classification
systems developed by South (1977). Analysis of the recovered materials assigned artifacts to one
of four primary categories—Activities, Architectural, Domestic (including Furnishings), and
Personal (see Appendix A). The Activities category includes nonhousehold items, transportation
items, farm-related equipment, tools, and firearms. The Architectural category includes all items
related to buildings such as brick, mortar, plaster, nails, window glass, and electrical items. The
Domestic category designation is used for items related to food service (tableware) and food
storage (including food preparation and tin cans); a Furnishings classification, a subcategory of
Domestic, encompasses household items such as furniture, stove parts, and lamp glass. The
Personal category generally contains items of individual use such as clothing, buttons, shoes,
dolls, and smoking pipes. Those artifacts such as unidentifiable pieces of glass and metal that
cannot be specifically placed in one of the above major categories are classified as Indeterminate.

Artifact Analysis Database
Various observations for each artifact classification mentioned above were recorded and entered
into an ACCESS database format on an IBM network system. In addition, computer printouts of
all data were compiled and archived with the permanent project files. The results of the analyses
of this collection are presented with the descriptions for each site in the following chapters, and
the raw data are presented in Appendix A.

36

CHAPTER 5
RESULTS OF INVESTIGATIONS

This chapter presents the results of the cultural resources survey and assesses the effects of the
proposed for proposed improvements to the Pickering Basin and the development of the
Industrial Park South on cultural resources in the vicinity. Management considerations and
recommendations based on the findings of this survey are presented in the final chapter.

BACKGROUND AND ARCHIVAL RESEARCH RESULTS
Background research and review of THC records indicated that eight previous cultural resources
investigations have been conducted within a 1.6-km (1-mi) radius of the proposed project areas
(see Figure 7). These previous surveys documented seven prehistoric archeological sites
(41VT79, 41VT94, 41VT98, 41VT99, 41VT101, 41VT102, and 41VT103) and one historic site
(41VT113) within the search radius (see Table 3). With the exception of site 41VT98, the
proposed project will have no adverse effect on these previously recorded archeological sites
because they are beyond the current APE of the proposed projects. Site 41VT98 extends into the
proposed Industrial Park South APE and was revisited as part of this survey effort.
Archival research focused on the historic period settlement in the immediate vicinity of the
proposed Industrial Park South project area. The project area is located on the Polito Castillo
league, originally consisting of 4,428 acres granted by the Mexican government in 1833 to Polito
Castillo, a colonist of the De Leon Colony (GLO 2012). The original 4,428-acre league has been
divided several times since 1845. The 1864 von Rosenberg map of the San Antonio and
Colorado rivers indicates little development had occurred in this area prior to the mid-nineteenth
century. The first major division of the Polito Castillo league occurred in 1881, when the Peter
Byrne estate was distributed to his heirs. After 1881, the land continued to be sold and
subdivided for farming, ranching, and domestic purposes.
In 1881, ownership of the Polito Castillo league was divided in four parts: the north half of the
north half owned by Peter Byrne; the south half of the north half owned by Patrick Hughes; the
north half of the south half owned by Peter Byrne; and the south half of the south half owned by
Winn Traylor. Although it is unknown when the lower three quarters of the land were sold, it is
known that prior to 1881, Peter Byrne established a homestead on the west half of the north half
37

of the south half. Peter Byrne maintained ownership of both quarters of the league until his death
in ca. 1881, at which time the County Court of Victoria County distributed his estate. The
distribution of the Peter Byrne estate caused the land to be further subdivided. The land located
on the north half of the north half of the original league was labeled Block One and divided into
five long lots. Each lot began on the east bank of the Guadalupe River and extended to the
eastern boundary line of the Polito Castillo league. These five lots were granted to (from north to
south) Ida Byrne, Walter Byrne, Eugene Byrne, H. M. Bingham (widow of Peter Byrne), and
Robert Byrne. The land on the north half of the south half of the league was split into three
sections labeled Block Two, H. M. Bingham 200-acre homestead, and Block Three. Block Two
was divided into five long lots extending from the east bank of the Guadalupe River east to the
west boundary line of the 200-acre homestead granted to H. M. Bingham. The five lots within
Block Two were granted to (from north to south) H. M. Bingham, Ida Byrne, Eugene Byrne,
Walter Byrne, and Robert Byrne. Block Three, also divided into five long lots, extended from the
east boundary line of the 200-acre homestead east to the eastern boundary line of the Polito
Castillo league. These five lots were granted to (from north to south) H. M. Bingham, Ida Byrne,
Eugene Byrne, Walter Byrne, and Robert Byrne (Figure 8; Victoria County Clerk [VCC]
1881:Deed Book [DB] 54:366–374).

Early twentieth-century development in the vicinity appears to have been sparse. In 1909, A.
Levi and Company attempted to establish a subdivision in the Placedo/Dacosta area with a series
of 40-acre lots extending along what is now DuPont Road to the Guadalupe River; however, the
venture never came to fruition. The 1927 United States Department of Agriculture (USDA)
Victoria County 1:63,000-scale soil map depicts several structures within the proposed Industrial
Park South project area, primarily along the now defunct Bloomington Road that extends across
the upland portion of the project area. By the mid-twentieth century, several additional structures
existed along Bloomington Road, as depicted on the 1951 United States Geological Survey
(USGS) Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic map.

CULTURAL RESOURCES SURVEY RESULTS
The proposed project areas were intensively investigated to determine the presence of cultural
resources using the field methods described in the preceding chapter. The 7.6-acre Pickering
Basin project area and the 1,390-acre Industrial Park South project area were investigated during
this cultural resources survey. Eight backhoe trenches and two shovel tests were excavated
within the Pickering Basin project area. Twenty-nine backhoe trenches and 682 shovel tests were
excavated in the Industrial Park South project area.

Pickering Basin
In the Pickering Basin project area, two trenches were excavated at each of the two proposed oil
dock ramps (Backhoe Trenches [BHTs] 1–4), and four trenches (BHTs 5–8) were excavated
within the area proposed for the potential expansion of Pickering Basin (Figure 9). The trenches
measured approximately 6 m (20 ft) in length and 2 m (6 ft) in width and were excavated until
sterile subsoil or gleyed deposits were encountered, or until collapsing sand deposits prevented
deeper excavation. Two shovel tests (Shovel Tests [STs] 1 and 2) were then excavated in the
proposed soil disposal area. The shovel test units were excavated to a depth of 80 cm below
surface (cmbs; 32 in below surface [inbs]).
BHTs 1 and 2 were excavated near the central portion of the existing barge slip where an oil dock
ramp leading down to the slip is proposed (Figure 10). The soil profiles encountered in the
trenches were very similar and consisted of an upper layer of sandy fill with a dense
concentration of recently imported chert cobbles between 0 and 50 cmbs (0 and 20 inbs)
underlain by naturally deposited dense clay floodplain deposits grading from black (10YR 2/1) to
light yellowish brown (10YR 5/8) with depth. The color changes below the fill material in BHTs
1 and 2 were gradual, indicating long-term natural soil development as opposed to an abrupt
deposition that would indicate recent placement of individual layers of fill or spoil deposits.
Redoximorphic features consistent with extended periods of saturation were common between 50
and 280 cmbs (20 and 110 inbs), and sterile subsoil consisting of light yellowish brown (2.5Y
6/4) friable silty clay with abundant calcium carbonate masses was encountered below 280 cmbs
(110 inbs). No cultural materials or evidence of buried, low terraces were found in the trenches.
BHTs 3 and 4 were excavated at the location of the second proposed oil dock ramp on a ridgeline
above the floodplain encountered in BHTs 1 and 2 (Figure 11). Based on observations of the
soils eroding on the slopes leading down into the basin and on the basin side walls, it appears that
the Pickering Basin was cut out of a broad Pleistocene-age terrace capped with eroded Holocene
39

10. Location of BHTs 1 and 2 at proposed oil dock ramp, view to the northwest

Figure 10. Location of BHTs 1 and 2 at proposed oil dock ramp, view to the northwest.

11. Location of BHTs 3 and 4 at second proposed oil dock ramp, view to the northeast

Figure 11. Location of BHTs 3 and 4 at second proposed oil dock ramp, view to the northeast.

alluvium at the location of BHTs 3 and 4. The deposits encountered in BHTs 3 and 4 consisted of
a black (10YR 2/1) clay loam A-horizon between approximately 0 and 50 cmbs (0 and 20 inbs)
and grayish brown (10YR 5/2) to light olive brown (2.5Y 5/3) sterile clay with calcium carbonate
between approximately 50 and 180 cmbs (20 and 71 inbs). These horizons were underlain by
thick deposits of light gray (10YR 7/2) coarse sand with small polished limestone pebbles and
calcium carbonate inclusions to depths exceeding 400 cmbs (157 inbs), followed by a grayish
brown (2.5Y 5/2) gleyed clay loam with common pressure faces. The basal coarse sands and
gleyed deposits are consistent with the Pleistocene Beaumont or Lissie formations, indicating that
backhoe trench excavations reached deposits predating human occupation in the region. No
cultural materials were encountered in BHTs 3 and 4.
The remaining four backhoe trenches were spread evenly throughout the potential expansion area
of Pickering Basin (see Figure 9). BHT 5 was excavated below the ridge encountered in BHTs 3
and 4 and consisted of fill materials of large concrete chunks, nails, and iron fragments between
approximately 0 and 170 cmbs (0 and 67 inbs), underlain between 170 and 300 cmbs (67 and 118
inbs) by thick Pleistocene sand deposits similar to those encountered in BHTs 3 and 4. BHT 6
was excavated on the same landform as BHT 5 and also consisted of highly disturbed deposits
consistent with fill materials. Extremely dense masses of calcium carbonate intermixed with
heavily mottled clay were encountered to approximately 300 cmbs (118 inbs) and were underlain
by light brownish gray (10YR 6/2) sterile clay subsoil with brownish yellow (10YR 6/6) mottles,
slickensides, and calcium carbonate nodules between 300 and 330 cmbs (118 and 130 inbs). BHT
7 was excavated on a ridgetop adjacent to an existing oil pipeline. The profile encountered
seemed relatively intact and consisted of brown (10YR 4/3) to strong brown (7.5YR 4/6) sandy
loam and sandy clay loam between 0 and 90 cmbs (0 and 35 inbs), and light gray (2.5Y 7/2) clay
with calcium carbonate masses and redox features between 90 and 130 cmbs (35 and 51 inbs).
These horizons were then underlain by pale brown (10YR 6/3) fine loose sand between 130 and
250 cmbs (51 and 98 inbs) and light gray (2.5Y 7/2) clay with abundant calcium carbonate
masses between 250 and 300 cmbs (98 and 118 inbs). The trench excavation was terminated at
300 cmbs (118 inbs) because it was clear that sterile subsoil deposits had been encountered. BHT
8 was excavated below the ridge southeast of BHT 6. The profile consisted of very pale brown
(10YR 8/3) sandy loam between 0 and 30 cmbs, yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) clay intermixed
with dark yellowish brown (10YR 4/6) clay between 30 and 300 cmbs (0 and 118 inbs), and
yellowish brown (10YR 5/4) clay with calcium carbonate masses between 300 and 330 cmbs
(118 and 130 inbs). The trench was terminated with the encounter of sterile subsoil clay deposits
between 300 and 330 cmbs (118 and 130 inbs). No cultural materials were encountered in BHTs
5–8.
Finally, two shovel tests (STs 1 and 2) were excavated in the 30.5-x-30.5-m (100-x-100-ft) soil
disposal area located adjacent to the northeastern corner of the potential expansion area of
Pickering Basin (see Figure 9). The soils encountered in STs 1 and 2 were similar to the upper
portion of the BHT 7 profile and consisted of brown (10YR 4/3) sandy loam between
approximately 0 and 40 cmbs (0 and 16 inbs), dark brown (7.5YR 3/4) sandy clay loam between
approximately 40 and 70 cmbs (16 and 27 inbs), and strong brown (7.5YR 4/6) sandy loam
between approximately 70 and 80 cmbs (27 and 31 inbs). No cultural materials were encountered
in either shovel test.

42

Industrial Park South
The Industrial Park South project area was separated into survey areas based on geomorphology
and the probability of archeological site occurrence, each with survey strategies reflecting the
probability of cultural material. The Guadalupe River floodplain survey area encompasses
approximately 175 acres of the Industrial Park South project area, the Guadalupe River valley
margin encompasses approximately 255 acres, and the Pleistocene uplands encompass the
remaining 960 acres of the project area (Figure 12).

Guadalupe River Floodplain
To determine the presence of deeply buried archeological deposits in the Holocene alluvium of
the Guadalupe River floodplain, backhoe trenching was conducted. For purposes of this project,
the floodplain was defined as areas below an elevation of 6.1 m (20 ft) amsl. Approximately 175
acres of the proposed Industrial Park South project area occur within the Guadalupe River
floodplain. Twenty-nine backhoe trenches were semi-systematically excavated across the project
area, with 25 trenches excavated within the floodplain and an additional four trenches excavated
along lower Guadalupe River valley margin landforms (Figure 13). Where vegetation and land
use allowed, trenches were excavated along transects extending from the Victoria Barge Canal to
the lower slopes of the Guadalupe River valley margin.
Soil profiles exposed within the backhoe trenches excavated in the floodplain were generally
similar, with two areas of comparable soils noted. The majority of the backhoe trenches across
the floodplain were characterized by a surface layer of very dark gray (10YR 3/1) silty clay with
subangular blocky structure transitioning gradually to dark gray (10YR 4/1) silty clay by depths
ranging from 50 to 80 cmbs (20 to 31 inbs), in turn gradually transitioning to light gray (10YR
7/1) clay extending to depths up to 4 m (13 ft). Within these soils, vertical burrows, carbonate
nodules, slickensides, and faint oxidation mottling were ubiquitous. In the easternmost portion of
the floodplain, BHTs 1–3 and 6–8 exhibited a surface horizon of very dark gray (10YR 3/1) to
gray (10YR 5/1) silty clay gradually transitioning to gray (10YR 6/1) clay with carbonate
concretions, vertical burrows, and faint mottling increasing with depth. Soils in this area were
typically saturated within 1–2 m (3.2–6.4 ft) of the surface and represent lacustrine sediments
deposited within the Atascosa Lagoon prior to its infilling and surface modification by modern
agricultural practices.
In addition to the two general areas described above, several unique soil profiles were
encountered during this survey. In BHT 2, thin layers of light yellowish brown (10YR 6/4) very
fine sandy clay loam were evident between 75 and 82 cmbs (29 and 32 inbs), likely representing
episodes of erosion from the valley margin into the former Atascosa Lagoon. A buried alluvial
fan deposit was encountered in BHT 5, characterized by dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty clay
loam gradually transitioning to very pale brown (10YR 7/4) medium sand at a depth of 36 cmbs
(14 inbs), which formed a distinct boundary over black (10YR 2/1) clay with common carbonate
concretions. BHT 10 was excavated on a low rise generally parallel to the floodplain margin in
the east-central portion of the floodplain. The rise, approximately 1–2 m (3.2–6.4 ft) higher than
the surrounding floodplain, consisted of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) fine loamy sand
gradually transitioning to dark yellowish brown (10YR 4/4) fine loamy sand at a depth of
approximately 40 cmbs (16 in), overlying a discontinuous calcic horizon with a maximum
thickness of 20 cm (8 in). The calcic horizon, in turn, overlies a thick (2.5 m [8.2 ft]) very pale
43

brown (10YR 8/2) loamy sand deposit characteristic of Deweyville terraces (Aiuvalasit 2006:37–
44). BHT 15, excavated near the floodplain margin in the central portion of the project area,
exhibited a typical surface layer of very dark gray (10YR 3/1) silty clay gradually transitioning to
dark gray (10YR 4/1) silty clay with few carbonate concretions by a depth of 70 cmbs (27 inbs).
These typical floodplain deposits, however, overlie dark gray (10YR 4/1) coarse sandy clay loam
that gradually transitions to light gray (10YR 7/1) coarse sandy clay loam with strong brown
(7.5YR 5/6) and reddish yellow (7.5YR 6/8) mottles. This soil profile likely indicates the
location of a former channel of the Guadalupe River, possibly dating to the late Pleistocene or
early Holocene (Weinstein 1992:Figure 10-2). An abandoned channel, approximately 20 ft (6 m)
in width, was indicated in this general vicinity in GLO survey data during the mid-nineteenth
century. The channel has been obscured by historic and recent agriculture and is no longer
evident on the surface. This survey resulted in the documentation of one historic period
archeological site (41VT167, discussed below).

Guadalupe River Valley Margin
The Guadalupe River valley margin encompasses approximately 255 acres of the proposed
Industrial Park South project area. Based on geomorphology and the locations of previously
recorded archeological sites in the region, the Guadalupe River valley margin is believed to hold
the highest probability of containing archeological sites, particularly locations of prehistoric
inhabitation, resource extraction, and mortuary sites. Four backhoe trenches, excavated during
the investigations of the Holocene floodplain deposits, extend to the lower slopes of the
Guadalupe River valley margin (see Figure 13). These trenches provided insight to the soils
encountered along the valley margin.
BHTs 4 and 9 were excavated along the toe slopes of the Guadalupe River valley margin. BHT 4
exhibited very dark gray (10YR 3/1) silty clay and silty clay loam overlying yellow (10YR 7/6)
clay loam. BHT 9 exhibited dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) fine sandy loam and dark yellowish
brown (10YR 4/4) fine loamy sand overlying very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) sandy clay. It
is likely that the underlying Pleistocene Beaumont Formation was encountered in both of these
trenches. BHT 25 was excavated at a valley margin toe slope below site 41VT98 to determine if
cultural deposits, buried by alluvial or colluvial processes, existed in this location. The soil
profile exposed in BHT 25 indicated that the uppermost 30–45 cm (12–18 in) has been disturbed,
probably by construction associated with the Victoria Barge Canal. These disturbed soils overlie
grayish brown (10YR 5/2) fine sandy loam to a depth of approximately 120 cmbs (47 inbs) where
a 20-cm-thick (6-in) light gray sandy loam horizon separates the overlying soil from gray (10YR
5/1) clay loam. BHT 29 was excavated on a toe slope above an unnamed ephemeral drainage
extending southeast toward an intermittent stream draining the uplands. This trench exhibits a
complex depositional history. The surface layer consisted of a 60-cm-thick (24-in) layer of very
pale brown (10YR 7/4) medium loamy sand gradually transitioning to a 12-cm-thick (5-in) layer
of very pale brown (10YR 8/2) medium loamy sand overlying a dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2)
medium sandy clay loam with red (2.5YR 5/8) mottles from 72 to 118 cmbs (28 to 46 inbs). A
brown (7.5YR 4/4) medium loamy sand with strong brown (7.5YR 5/8) mottles was encountered
from 118 to 300 cmbs (46 to 118 inbs), which gradually transitioned to brown (7.5YR 4/4) coarse
sandy clay loam. The sandy constituents of this profile indicate deposition of sandy soils from
the adjacent uplands to the west, but the dark grayish brown sandy clay loam horizon is likely to
have derived from alluvial deposition of eroded clay-rich Beaumont Formation deposits from the
uplands to the east.
46

The primary survey method used across this portion of the survey area was shovel testing.
Shovel tests were systematically excavated in 100-m (323-ft) intervals across valley margin
landforms at locations of surface artifacts and at map-documented locations of historic period
structures (Figure 14). A variety of landforms and soil types were encountered during the cultural
resources survey in this area. The westernmost portion of the survey area consists of a generally
level upland escarpment slightly lower in elevation than the adjacent Pleistocene uplands and
dissected by several ephemeral drainages (see Chapter 2). This area, mantled by deep loamy sand
deposits, likely represents a High Deweyville terrace with a mantle of Holocene sand deposited
by eolian processes. The central and eastern portions of the valley margin exhibited more
variable soil conditions, with some areas mantled by sandy loams and others by clay or clay
loams, largely dictated by the deposition and erosion of eolian sands during the Holocene. In
total, 376 shovel tests were excavated across the Guadalupe River valley margin. This survey
resulted in the recording of four archeological sites (41VT156, 41VT158, 41VT165, and
41VT166) and five isolates (Isolates 9–13) and the documentation of one historic period cemetery
(41VT168) location. In addition, the boundary of one previously recorded site (41VT98) was
extended.

Pleistocene Uplands
The Pleistocene uplands encompass approximately 960 acres of the proposed Industrial Park
South project area. Based on geomorphology and the locations of previously recorded
archeological sites in the region, the Pleistocene uplands hold the lowest probability of containing
prehistoric archeological sites; however, numerous historic period sites, particularly those dating
to the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, are likely to exist. As with the Guadalupe
River valley margin, the primary survey method used across this portion of the project area was
shovel testing. Shovel tests were systematically excavated in 110-m (360-ft) intervals across the
majority of the survey area at locations of surface artifacts and at map-documented locations of
historic period structures (see Figure 14). In total, 304 shovel tests were excavated across the
Pleistocene uplands. This survey resulted in the recording of eight historic period archeological
sites (41VT155–41VT162, 41VT163, and 41VT164) and eight isolates (Isolates 1–4 and Isolates
14–17).

Site 41VT155
Site 41VT155 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to mid twentieth-century domestic site
located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies the northwestern corner of
an agricultural field situated on a level, featureless portion of the upland coastal plain along the
now defunct Bloomington Road (Figure 15). Presently wooded, the site is encompassed by an
active agricultural field (Figure 16). Surface artifacts were exposed within the wooded area and
within adjacent portions of the plowed field, with artifact density rapidly decreasing with distance
into the plowed field. Twelve shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary, in
conjunction with the surface artifact scatter. Four positive shovel tests occupy the central portion
of the wooded area. Site 41VT155 encompasses approximately 1.5 acres, measuring
approximately 90 m (295 ft) north-to-south by 90 m (295 ft) east-to-west at an elevation of
19.5 m (62 ft) amsl. Several areas of recently discarded items (televisions, modern bottles and
cans) were also observed at the site. Three dilapidated and collapsing outbuildings, likely barns
and a shed, were observed and photodocumented at the site (Figures 17–19).
47

Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site occupies an area near the transition between Dacosta sandy clay loam and
Laewest clay; however, the soils encountered during this survey suggest that the site is entirely
within the Laewest clay map unit.
Numerous historic period surface artifacts were observed across site 41VT155 and were
concentrated in the central, wooded, portion of the site. Surface artifacts consisted primarily of
refined earthenware and bottle glass.
The collected artifact assemblage encountered at the site consists of historic period domestic
items recovered in four shovel tests at depths ranging from 0–20 cmbs (0–8 inbs) (n=31) and
from the general site surface (n=21), for a total of 52 artifacts: Domestic (n=31), Architectural
(n=2), Furnishing (n=4), Personal (n=1), and Indeterminate (n=14) items. The Domestic artifacts
are further subdivided into ceramics (n=18) and glass (n=13). The refined earthenwares consist
of blue-tinted, high-fired ironstone (n=5; 1850–1910), porcelain (n=1), flow blue (n=1; 1830–
1860), light blue tinted whiteware (n=2; 1880–1930), pure white whiteware (n=2; post-1890), and
light ivory tinted whiteware (n=1; post-1920). Utilitarian ceramics are Bristol glazed
interior/exterior stoneware (n=2; post-1900), natural clay slipped interior/Bristol and natural clay
slipped exterior stoneware (n=1; 1890–1915), natural clay slipped interior/exterior (n=1; 1875–
1900), and yellowware (n=2). Domestic glass consists of amber/brown (n=2), aqua (n=1), ashtint (n=4; post-1915), colorless (n=2; post-1880), and manganese/solarized (n=2; 1870s–1920)
body sherds, as well as non-applied, turn-molded clear and aqua bottle lips (1880–1910).
Architectural items are a wire nail (post-1890) and a piece of aqua window glass. Furnishings
include three glass fragments related to electrical lamps: two clear light bulb fragments and a
portion of an electric lamp base. The remaining Furnishings artifact is a piece of table glass that
has melted but was probably originally oval shaped. Three sides appear to have original edges,
but the fourth is broken. One edge has two thick beads that are present on both sides of the
artifact. The two remaining edges have thickened segments that are roughly rectangular in cross
section and irregular in depth. The thickened segments are only visible on one side of the artifact.
Finally, there is a series of wavy, ridged lines on the interior of the artifact that occur on the same
side as the thickened segments. The Personal artifact is a porcelain four-hole button. The
Indeterminate artifacts are thin iron fragments (n=10), modern plastic fragments (n=3), and a
possible reflector fragment. The possible reflector is a piece of red glass with large knobs
approximately 9 mm in diameter and 5 mm in height protruding from one surface. It does not
appear to match any Depression glass or pattern glass designs and is likely a reflector fragment.
A primary structure is depicted at the location of site 41VT155 on the 1927 USDA Victoria
County 1:63,000-scale soils map and the 1951 USGS Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic
map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the property.
Site 41VT155 is located on the west half of the north half of the north half of the Polito Castillo
league, on a portion of land that was known as Lot One of Block One and granted to Ida Byrne by
the Victoria County Court in 1881. Ida Byrne maintained ownership of the land until 1897, at
which time she sold Lots One, Two, Three, and Four of Block One (850 acres) to W. P. Stafford
and C. L. Thurmond (VCC 1897:DB 36:516). Between 1897 and 1924 the property was granted
to L. G. Kreisle, who owned the land until his death in 1924. After the death of L. G. Kreisle, the
land was willed to his brother Emil Kreisle of San Antonio (VCC 1924:DB 105:29). Emil

Kreisle continued to maintain a permanent residence in San Antonio until his death in 1945, but
according to his obituary he was devoted to farming and ranching in his later years (San Antonio
Express 17 November 1945). Between 1924 and 1945, Emil Kreisle granted the property to his
daughter Myrtle Kreisle Trip, who in 1974 granted the property to her son Gustav T. Trip Jr.
(VCC 1974:DB 860:476). In 1975, Gustav T. Trip Jr. granted the property to his cousins Lexina
K. Miguez and Lissette Trip Wilson, who in 1977 sold the property to the Gulf Oil Corporation of
Pennsylvania (VCC 1975:DB 869:255; VCC 1977:DB 942:655). Between 1977 and 1980, the
Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito Castillo league and formed one
tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres. In 1984, the Gulf Oil Corporation sold the property to
Ralph R. Gilster, Jr., D. H. Braman, Jr., Dennis O’Connor, and Tom O’Connor, Jr., trustees for
the Kate S. O’Connor Trust for Junie O’Connor Gilster, Ralph Richard Gilster III, Kathryn

Virginia Drake Lebermann (VCC 1984:DB 1248:772). Finally, in 2007, Ralph R. Gilster, Jr.,
D.H. Braman, Jr., Dennis O’Connor, and Tom O’Connor, Jr., (trustees) sold the property to the
current owner, Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC
2012:DB 3914:17).
Site 41VT155 likely represents the location of a late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century
farmstead. Like many of the historic period farmsteads in the region, the site may have been
occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner; thus, determining the precise
occupant at a given period is problematic. Demolition of the associated structures has adversely
affected the site’s integrity of design and materials. Given the site’s minimal, fragmentary, and
redundant artifact content, and the lack of architectural features, coupled with disturbances to the
site by modern agriculture, 41VT155 is considered to have little research potential. As such, the
site is not likely to yield any additional information relevant to understanding rural domestic life
or community and regional development in the Guadalupe River valley of the central Texas
coastal region. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT155 is not eligible for inclusion in the
NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an SAL under 13 TAC
26.8. No further investigations are recommended at site 41VT155 for purposes of developing the
proposed Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

Site 41VT156
Site 41VT156 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century domestic site,
with a minor, nondiagnostic prehistoric component, located within proposed Industrial Park
South APE. The site occupies a prominent ridge extending southwest from the upland
escarpment (Figure 20). Two two-track roads intersect near the center of the site, where a
modern windmill and two concrete stock tanks are located (Figure 21). Observed surface
artifacts were exposed within each of the two-track roads and were concentrated at the
escarpment and along the northwestern slope of the landform. Thirty-six shovel tests were
excavated to define the site boundary. Seven positive shovel tests occupy the highest and most
level portion of the landform. Site 41VT156 encompasses approximately 3.6 acres, measuring
roughly 145 m (476 ft) north-to-south by 175 m (574 ft) east-to-west at an elevation ranging from
14–18 m (46–59 ft) amsl. The site is primarily covered by pasture.

54

21. Field photograph of site 41VT156, view to the northeast

Figure 21. Field photograph of site 41VT156, view to the northeast.

Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by a fine sandy loam
that gradually transitions from very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) to dark yellowish brown
(10YR 3/4) at the surface to a subsoil ranging from dark yellowish brown (10YR 3/6) to brownish
yellow (10YR 6/6). Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the Natural Resources
Conservation Service (NRCS 2012), the site occupies an area near the transition between Runge
fine sandy loam and Laewest clay; however, the soils encountered during this survey suggest that
the site is entirely within the Runge fine sandy loam map unit.
Surface artifacts were observed in several eroded areas of the site but were concentrated within
the portion of the roadway bisecting the escarpment in the southwestern area of the site and
within the roadway descending the northwestern slope of the landform. Surface artifacts
observed at site 41VT156 consisted of 12 prehistoric artifacts and 45 historic period artifacts.
Prehistoric surface artifacts were concentrated in the southwest portion of the site and consisted
primarily of chipped lithic debitage (n=11) and a single fragment of burned rock. Historic period
surface artifacts consisted of manganese/solarized, ash-tint, colorless, amber, aqua, cobalt, and
black bottle glass (n=22), undecorated refined earthenware (n=14), window glass (n=3), one
fragment of coarse earthenware, a cut nail, a fragment of cast iron, a pocket knife, a cartridge
case, and an unidentifiable lead mass. The single oyster shell observed on the surface could not
be positively associated with either the prehistoric or historic component of the site.
The subsurface artifact assemblage encountered at site 41VT156 consists of a single piece of
chert shatter (0–10 cmbs [0–4 inbs]) from ST C-5 (10–30 cmbs [4–12 inbs]), as well as historic
period items (0–40 cmbs [0–16 inbs]) recovered in seven shovel tests. Thirty-six historic artifacts
56

were collected, consisting of Domestic (n=18), Architectural (n=7), Activities (n=1), Furnishing
(n=1), Personal (n=1), and Indeterminate (n=6) artifacts. The Domestic ceramics consist of
porcelain (n=2), blue-tinted, high-fired ironstone (n=1; 1850–1910), pure white whiteware (n=2;
post-1890), and coarse earthenware (n=1) with a natural clay slipped interior and salt glazed
exterior and cobalt blue decoration (1840–1900). The Domestic glass items are amber/brown
body sherds (n=3), ash-tint body sherds (n=2; post-1915), and colorless body sherds (n=4; post1880). A fragment of an amber/brown bottle base is probably from a square or rectangularshaped panel bottle and exhibits a rounded depression in the interior of the base. The artifact is
heavily weathered; thus, no mold seams could be observed. An amber/brown bottle lip fragment
was also collected, with an apparent Brandy finish. A piece of manganese/solarized table glass
exhibits a molded facet and a series of molded ribs approximately 3 millimeters (mm) wide. The
seven Architectural artifacts consist of cut nails (n=5; 1840–1880), a wire nail (post-1890), and a
piece of light green window glass. The Activities-related artifact is a portion of a shotgun shell
base or rimmed center-fire cartridge. Unfortunately, the artifact was slightly deformed and the
mouth was no longer present, making identification more difficult. In addition, no headstamp
was discerned on the artifact. The internal diameter measured between .40 and .42 inches,
consistent with a .410 shotgun shell base or a variety of straight-case .40-caliber rifle cartridges.
The Furnishing-related artifact is a piece of ash-tint lamp glass (post-1915). The Personal artifact
is a fragment of a double-sided, 78-rpm record (1915–1955) that appears to be burned. The
Indeterminate artifacts are wire (n=1), thin iron fragments (n=4), and ash-tint angular glass
fragments.
A primary structure is depicted at the location of site 41VT156 on the 1927 USDA Victoria
County 1:63,000-scale soils map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the
ownership of the property. Archival research indicated that site 41VT156 is located on the west
half of the south half of the north half of the Polito Castillo league. In 1881, this quarter of the
league was owned by Patrick Hughes, who arrived in Victoria in 1852 and quickly established
himself as a rancher. In 1865, Patrick Hughes married Eliza Traylor, the daughter of Winn
Traylor. Eliza Traylor died the following year. Prior to her death, she gave birth to the couple’s
only child, Eliza Hughes, who in 1882 married J. J. Welder (Victoria Advocate 12 May 1968a).
After the death of his wife, Patrick Hughes continued to live on and maintain his ranch until
1884, at which time he sold the property to Henry C. Traylor. In 1891, Henry C. Traylor divided
the land and granted 395.3 acres to his son Winn Traylor, who in 1912 sold 200 acres to Charles
G. Taylor, and being the same property on which site 41VT156 is located. Charles G. Taylor
maintained ownership until 1919, at which time he sold the land to C. E. Stubblefield and his wife
Elizabeth Stubblefield (Gaddus et al. 1999:274). C. E. Stubblefield maintained ownership of the
property until his death in 1952, at which time it was willed to his wife Elizabeth Stubblefield,
who in 1977 sold the property to the Gulf Oil Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1953:DB
350:79; VCC 1977:DB 942:629). Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation
consolidated several tracts from the Polito Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing
1,955.21 acres that is now under the ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation
(VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC 2012:DB 3914:17).
Site 41VT156 likely represents the location of a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century
farmstead, with a minor prehistoric component. Like many of the historic period farmsteads in
the region, the site may have been occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner;
thus, determining the occupant at a given period is problematic. Demolition and neglect of the
associated structures has adversely affected the site’s integrity of design and materials. Given the
site’s minimal, fragmentary, and redundant artifact content, and the lack of architectural features,
57

coupled with disturbances to the site by modern ranching, 41VT156 is considered to have little
research potential. As such, the site is not likely to yield any additional information relevant to
understanding rural domestic life or community and regional development in the Guadalupe
River valley of the central Texas coastal region. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT156 is
not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for
designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further investigations are recommended at site

Site 41VT157
Site 41VT157 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century domestic site
located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies a small pasture area
situated on a level, featureless portion of the upland coastal plain located along the now defunct
Bloomington Road (Figure 22). Surface artifacts were exposed around a barn and within an
unnamed two-track road paralleling a drainage ditch that forms the northern edge of the site.
Nine shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary, in conjunction with the surface
artifact scatter. Two positive shovel tests occupy the central portion of the pasture. Site
41VT157 encompasses approximately 1.4 acres, measuring approximately 80 m (262 ft) north-tosouth by 100 m (328 ft) east-to-west at an elevation of 19.5 m (62 ft) amsl. A barn, currently in
use for modern ranching purposes, is located along the edge of the pasture on the western edge of
the site (Figure 23).
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark brown
(10YR 2/2) sandy clay loam that gradually transitions to dark grayish brown clay loam by depths
of approximately 40–50 cm (16–20 in). Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site occupies an area of Telferner series fine sandy loam. The soils
encountered during this survey suggest that the site is within the Telferner fine sandy loam map
unit.
Observed surface artifacts consisted entirely of historic-period Domestic artifacts: undecorated
refined earthenware (n=4), porcelain (n=1), and a cut rib bone. The subsurface artifact
assemblage encountered at site 41VT157 consists of five historic period domestic items recovered
in one shovel test (Shovel Test [ST] WH-2) at depths from 0–30 cmbs (0–12 inbs) and a single
mass of concrete (not collected) encountered in ST C-1. Five historic artifacts were collected
from site 41VT157, all consisting of Domestic items: two pieces of blue-tinted, high-fired
ironstone (1850–1910), two ash-tint glass body sherds (post-1915), and one aqua glass body
sherd.
A primary structure is depicted at the location of site 41VT157 on the 1927 USDA Victoria
County 1:63,000-scale soils map and the 1951 USGS Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic
map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the property.
Site 41VT157 is located on the west half of the north half of the south half of the Polito Castillo
league, along the west side of Old Bloomington Road. This tract of land was known as the H. M.
Bingham 200-acre homestead in 1881, and was owned by H. M. Bingham, the widow of Peter
Byrne. Between 1881 and 1920, H. M. Bingham granted the property to Ida Byrne, who
maintained ownership of the homestead until her death in 1920. After Ida Byrne’s death, the
property was willed to her niece Elizabeth Woodhouse, who sold the portion of the tract on the
58

23. Barn associated with 41VT157, view to the south-southeast

Figure 23. Barn associated with 41VT157, view to the south-southeast.

west side of Old Bloomington Road to C. H. Stiernberg and D. D. Spencer (VCC 1920:Probate
Minutes [PM] 16:589; VCC 1945:DB 209:56). In 1960, C. H. Stiernberg and D. D. Spencer sold
the property to Joseph B. Bumgardner, who in 1977 sold the property to the Gulf Oil Corporation
of Pennsylvania (VCC 1960:DB 517:673; VCC 1977:DB 943:315). Between 1977 and 1980, the
Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito Castillo league and formed one
tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the ownership of the Victoria County
Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC 2012:DB 3914:17).
Site 41VT157 likely represents the location of an early- to mid-twentieth-century farmstead. As
with many of the historic period farmsteads in the region, the site may have been occupied by a
tenant farmer rather than the property owner; thus, determining the occupant at a given period is
problematic. The relocation and repurposing of the associated structure has adversely affected
the site’s integrity of design and setting. Given the site’s minimal, fragmentary, and redundant
artifact content, coupled with disturbances to the site by modern ranching and agriculture,
41VT157 is considered to have little research potential. As such, the site is not likely to yield any
additional information relevant to understanding rural domestic life or community and regional
development in the Guadalupe River valley of the central Texas coastal region. Thus, it is
recommended that site 41VT157 is not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria
enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further
investigations are recommended at site 41VT157 for purposes of developing the proposed
Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

60

Site 41VT158
Site 41VT158 consists of a prehistoric habitation site largely disturbed by a historic period
component located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies the escarpment
of the Guadalupe River valley, with the remnants of a prehistoric midden occupying a lower slope
bench of the valley margin, which has been disturbed by Stubblefield Road (Figure 24). Seventythree shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary, in conjunction with the surface
artifact scatter. Seventeen positive shovel tests span from the summit to the toe slope of the
landform, with eroded artifacts extending to the floodplain below the site (Figure 25). Site
41VT158 encompasses approximately 4.8 acres, measuring approximately 300 m (984 ft)
northeast-to-southwest by 150 m (492 ft) northwest-to-southeast at elevations ranging from 5 m
(16 ft) amsl at the floodplain to 18 m (59 ft) amsl at the summit of the landform. A small,
corrugated metal shed and several fences occupy the midslope of the landform.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay loam. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site spans areas of Runge fine sandy loam at the summit of the landform,
Laewest clay along the slopes, and Meguin silty clay at the foot of the slope. The soils
encountered during this survey suggest that the sandy surface layer of the landform summit has
been eroded, most likely by historic agriculture.
Surface artifacts observed at the site consisted of both prehistoric and historic period artifacts.
Prehistoric artifacts observed on the site surface included chipped lithic debitage (n=18; seven
pieces collected), one biface fragment (collected), burned rock, burned bone, and burned brackish
shell and marine shell. The biface fragment is a possible medial section of a chert dart point that
shows evidence of fine retouching along the extant lateral edges and is well flaked on both faces
with a symmetrical cross section. These artifacts were observed in an area of dark soil exposed
within Stubblefield Road, which appears to have disturbed a prehistoric midden deposit (Figure
26). Organic-rich deposits extended approximately 180 m (590 ft) along Stubblefield Road, with
prehistoric lithic artifacts concentrated near the escarpment of the lower slope bench above the
floodplain. Historic period artifacts observed on the site surface included undecorated refined
earthenware (n=6), machine-made brick fragments (n=6), coarse earthenware sewer or drain pipe
(n=1; collected), and bottle glass (n=9) including colorless (n=1), aqua (n=3), cobalt (n=2), olive
green (n=1), black (n=1), and light green (n=1). Four surface artifacts were collected. One is a
clear glass bottle base with an Owens ring (post-1910). The light green bottle glass, a portion of a
panel bottle, is embossed “. . .McLEAN . . .” and probably represents DR. J. H. McLEAN'S
VOLCANIC OIL LINIMENT (post-1854; Hoolihan 2008:475–476). The fragment of sewer or
drainpipe exhibits a series of irregular, deeply incised annular lines on the exterior. The fourth
collected surface artifact is a piece of asbestos fragment that is curved and is therefore identified
as a piece of pipe lagging; the exterior is two-tone buff and green, suggesting that the artifact was
painted. Historic period artifacts were concentrated within Stubblefield Road near the bottom of
the slope.
The subsurface prehistoric artifact assemblage at site 41VT158 includes eight artifacts recovered
from eight shovel tests (from 0–20 cmbs [0–8 inbs]). Prehistoric artifacts consist of six pieces of
chipped lithic debitage, one possible expedient edge-modified flake tool, and one undecorated
sandy paste ceramic sherd with bone tempering. Prehistoric artifacts at the site appear to be
concentrated in two loci; one located on the lower slope bench above the floodplain and another
at the summit of the landform (see Figure 24). None of the prehistoric lithic artifacts recovered

from site 41VT158 were temporally or culturally diagnostic. The single ceramic sherd, recovered
from ST CG-14 at the summit of the landform, can be assigned to Weinstein’s (1992:89–95)
Goose Creek Plain, var. Traylor Ranch, category; however, the interpretive utility of this tentative
type-variety classification system is limited. The presence of this single ceramic sherd indicates
that the site may date to the early portion of the Late Prehistoric period (ca. A.D. 1000–
1200/1300) or include a Late Prehistoric component.
The collected subsurface historic period artifact assemblage from site 41VT158 includes 28
artifacts recovered in nine shovel tests (from 0–20 cmbs [0–8 inbs]): Domestic (n=16),
Architectural (n=4), Furnishings (n=1), Activities (n=2), and Indeterminate (n=5) artifacts.
Among the Domestic artifacts, only one ceramic was collected: a single fragment of buffware
with brown glazed exterior and brown slipped interior. Thirteen of the Domestic glass artifacts
are body sherds, consisting of amber/brown (n=1), amber/brown with stippling (post-1940), ashtint (n=4; post-1915), and colorless (n=7; post-1880). The remaining Domestic glass items are
two clear fragments of possible table glass. The Architectural artifacts are three wire nails (post1890) and a piece of light tinted plate glass. The two Activities-related artifacts are a fence staple
and possible rat trap mechanism (post-1894; Hooker 1894). The rat trap mechanism is composed
of a single strand of wire bent at a 90° angle, with a wire coil wrapped around the wire strand
next to the bend. Although this artifact is also consist with a piece of single strand barbed wire
(cf. Glidden's Coil, Hanging-barb One-Strand Variation or Glidden’s Barb, One Strand Variation
[Clifton 1970:30–31]), the bent, single strand wire makes the identification as a hammer and
spring from a traditional mouse or rat trap seem more likely. The single Furnishing-related
artifact is a piece of colorless lamp glass (post-1880). The Indeterminate artifacts are four pieces
of unidentified thin iron and an iron can lid fragment that was originally oval with a rolled rim.
No structures are depicted at the location of site 41VT158 on any of the historic maps reviewed
during this project. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the
property. Site 41VT158 is located on the west half of the north half of the north half of the Polito
Castillo league, on a portion of land that was known as Lot Five of Block One and granted to
Robert Byrne by the Victoria County Court in 1881. Between 1881 and 1897, Robert Byrne
granted the property to W. P. Stafford and C. L. Thurmond, who sold a 321-acre tract, containing
a portion of Lot Five, to Austin Hickey (VCC 1897:DB 38:623). The 321-acre tract was located
from the east bank of the Guadalupe River to the west side of the Old Bloomington Road along
the north boundary line of the Polito Castillo league with a south boundary line along
Stubblefield Road. Austin Hickey lived on the property and maintained ownership of the 321acre tract until his death in 1903 (Advocate 3 October 1903), at which time the land was passed to
his heirs Tina Hickey, Mary Hickey, Richard Hickey, Andrew Hickey, and Joseph Hickey. In
1905, the Austin Hickey heirs foreclosed on the property and the 321-acre tract was sold in a
Sheriff’s sale to Mary T. Glass, who sold the property to Adolph Fromme in 1906 (VCC
1905:DB 53:186; VCC 1906:DB 56:261). Adolph Fromme is known to have lived on the
property in the “Austin Hickey Place” in ca. 1910, and maintained ownership of the property until
his death in 1943 (Daily Advocate 4 November 1910). In 1943, the property was passed to
Adolph Fromme’s widow Teresa Fromme, who maintained ownership of the property until her
death in 1976 (Advocate 18 January 1976). After the death of Teresa Fromme, the property was
willed to her two surviving sisters Ernestine Ackermann and Elizabeth Ackermann, who in 1980
sold the property to the Gulf Oil Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1980:DB 1068:473).
Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito
Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the
ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC
2012:DB 3914:17).
64

Site 41VT158 represents a possible Late Prehistoric site with a late nineteenth- to mid-twentiethcentury domestic occupation. Unfortunately, the prehistoric component of the site has been
largely destroyed by ground-disturbing activities related to both the historic period component at
the site as well as the construction and maintenance of Stubblefield Road, which bisects the
prehistoric midden deposit on the lower slope bench. Although lithic debris and a variety of
burned faunal remains are clearly visible within Stubblefield Road, only two artifacts were
recovered from the 24 shovel tests excavated along the roadway (see Figure 24). It appears that
much of the midden deposit has been displaced during the process of grading the road. During
the historic period, the site may have been occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property
owner; thus, determining the occupant at a given period is problematic. The possible demolition
of any additional structures associated with the site has adversely affected the site’s integrity of
design and materials. Given the site’s minimal and fragmentary artifact content, coupled with
disturbances to the site by the current agricultural and ranching activities in the vicinity, 41VT158
is considered to have little research potential. As such, the site is not likely to yield any
additional information relevant to understanding rural domestic life or community and regional
development in the Guadalupe River valley of the central Texas coastal region. Thus, it is
recommended that site 41VT159 is not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria
enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further
investigations are recommended at site 41VT158 for purposes of developing the proposed
Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

Site 41VT159
Site 41VT159 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century domestic site
located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies a narrow pasture area
situated on a level, featureless portion of the upland coastal plain located adjacent to the now
defunct Bloomington Road (Figure 27). Ten shovel tests were excavated to define the site
boundary, in conjunction with the surface artifact scatter and architectural remains. Three
positive shovel tests occupy the northern portion of the pasture. Site 41VT159 encompasses
approximately 1.2 acres, measuring approximately 110 m (361 ft) northwest-to-southeast by 55 m
(180 ft) northeast-to-southwest at an elevation of 19 m (62 ft) amsl. Two small concrete
foundations are located in the central portion of the site and a small brick feature, possibly
capping a well, is located in the northeastern portion of the site (Figure 28). A shed, currently in
use, is situated near the northern end of the site (Figure 29) and two accumulations of historic and
modern domestic, agricultural, and industrial debris is located to the north and south of the shed.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site occupies an area of Laewest clay. The soils encountered during this
survey confirm this identification.
Surface artifacts observed at the site, in addition to those observed within piles of debris
previously mentioned, consisted of domestic artifacts, including undecorated refined earthenware
(n=2), molded refined earthenware (n=1), and a cobalt glass Vicks bottle. The bricks capping the
probable well are red-paste, dry-pressed, machine-made bricks manufactured by D’Hanis Brick
and Tile Company and bear the Common Brick Manufacturers Association symbol (c. 1920s;
Steinbomer 1982).

The subsurface artifact assemblage encountered at site 41VT159 includes a single core flake of
