remaining rivet head. This artifact is interpreted as a clothing rivet. The Indeterminate category
includes a heavily corroded piece of iron that might be part of a square rod; braided iron wire that
is probably a piece of wire rope; a fragment of iron sheet metal consisting of two pieces
connected with a locked seam and evidence of a third seam; eight unidentified thin iron
fragments; 12 iron wire fragments; and two pieces of modern plastic (post-1945).
Three structures are depicted at the location of site 41VT159 on the 1927 USDA Victoria County
1:63,000-scale soils map and the 1951 USGS Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic map.
Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the property. Site
41VT159 is located on the west half of the south half of the north half of the Polito Castillo
league. In 1881, this quarter of the league was owned by Patrick Hughes, who arrived in Victoria
in 1852, and quickly established himself as a rancher. In 1865, Patrick Hughes married Eliza
Traylor, the daughter of Winn Traylor. Eliza Traylor died the following year, but prior to her
death gave birth to the couple’s only child, Eliza Hughes, who in 1882 married J. J. Welder
(Victoria Advocate 12 May 1968a). After the death of his wife, Patrick Hughes continued to live
on and maintain his ranch until 1884, at which time he sold the property to Henry C. Traylor. In
1891, Henry C. Traylor divided the land and granted 395.3 acres to his son Winn Traylor, who in
1912 sold 200 acres to Charles G. Taylor, being the same property on which sites 41VT156 and
41VT159 are located. Charles G. Taylor maintained ownership until 1919, at which time he sold
the land to C. E. Stubblefield and his wife Elizabeth Stubblefield (Gaddus et al. 1999:274). C. E.
Stubblefield maintained ownership of the property until his death in 1952, at which time it was
willed to his wife Elizabeth Stubblefield, who in 1977 sold the property to the Gulf Oil
Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1953:DB 350:79; VCC 1977:DB 942:629). Between 1977
and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito Castillo league and
formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the ownership of the
Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC 2012:DB 3914:17).
Site 41VT159 likely represents the location of a late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century
farmstead. As with many of the historic period farmsteads in the region, the site may have been
occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner; thus, determining the occupant at a
given period is problematic. Demolition of the associated structures has adversely affected the
site’s integrity of design and materials. Given the site’s fragmentary and redundant artifact
content, coupled with disturbances to the site by the current agricultural and ranching activities in
the vicinity, 41VT159 is considered to have little research potential. As such, the site is not likely
to yield any additional information relevant to understanding rural domestic life or community
68

and regional development in the Guadalupe River valley of the central Texas coastal region.
Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT159 is not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the
criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further
investigations are recommended at site 41VT159 for purposes of developing the proposed
Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

Site 41VT160
Site 41VT160 consists of a late nineteenth to early twentieth century domestic site located within
proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies the northwestern portion of a pasture area
situated on a level, featureless portion of the upland coastal plain located near the now defunct
Old Bloomington Road (Figure 30). Surface artifacts were exposed in the central portion of the
site by animal (likely feral hog) rooting activity (Figure 31). Twenty-four shovel tests were
excavated to define the site boundary, in conjunction with the surface artifact scatter. Twelve
positive shovel tests occupy the northwestern portion of the pasture. Site 41VT160 encompasses
approximately 6.1 acres, measuring approximately 125 m (410 ft) northwest-to-southeast by
250 m (820 ft) northeast-to-southwest at an elevation of 19 m (62 ft) amsl. A Kinder Morgan
pipeline right-of-way intersects the site. Although currently in pasture, the field encompassing
the site was recently planted in corn, as evidenced by corn stubble and planting rows observed at
the site and in recent aerial imagery.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark brown
(10YR 2/2) sandy clay loam that gradually transitions to dark grayish brown clay loam by depths
of approximately 40–50 cm (16–20 in). Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site occupies an area of Telferner series fine sandy loam. The soils
encountered during this survey suggest that the site is within the Telferner fine sandy loam map
unit.
Surface artifacts observed at the site (n=17) consisted primarily of domestic artifacts, including
decorated (n=2) and undecorated refined earthenware (n=6), Bristol glazed interior/exterior
stoneware (n=2), porcelain (n=1), milk glass (n=1), and aqua glass (n=1), and architectural items
of red paste, machine-made brick fragments (n=2), a mortar fragment, and a piece of dressed
sandstone.
The collected artifact assemblage encountered at site 41VT160 includes 112 historic period
artifacts recovered in 12 shovel tests (0–40 cmbs [0–16 inbs]), as well as six artifacts from the
general surface for a total of 118 historic artifacts from the site. Historic material consists of
Domestic (n=79), Architectural (n=25), Furnishing (n=3), Activities (n=3), and Indeterminate
(n=8) items. The Domestic category is further subdivided into ceramic, glass, and metal artifacts.
The ceramics include blue-tinted, non-vitrified ironstone (n=1; 1850–1910); undecorated
porcelain (n=4) and porcelain with floral decalcomania decoration (n=1; 1890–1950); light blue
tinted whiteware (n=4; 1880–1930), pure white whiteware (n=4; post-1890), and a discolored
whiteware. The ironstone fragment (from the surface) has a partial maker’s mark stamped “. . .S
& G . . .” that might indicate either Evans & Glasson (1850–1862; Godden 1964:241)or an L. S.
& G. mark used by Rubian Art Pottery Ltd (1906–1930; Godden 1964:401, 455), but neither can
be definitively attributed to the sherd. Utilitarian ceramics include natural clay slipped
interior/Bristol exterior stoneware (n=1; 1890–1915), Bristol interior/exterior stoneware with
sponged cobalt decoration (n=1; post-1900), and a piece of buffware. The buffware sherd has a
69

31. Feral hog rooting activity and exposed artifacts at site 41VT160

Figure 31. Feral hog rooting activity and exposed artifacts at site 41VT160.

black paste, a brown glazed interior, and a reddish-brown slipped exterior. Of the 60 Domestic
glass items collected, 58 are from body sherds, while the remaining two are bottle lip and bottle
neck. The body sherds are amber/brown (n=13), aqua (n=4), ash-tint (n=8; post-1915), colorless
(n=22; post-1880), light green (n=1), manganese/solarized (n=2; 1870s–1920), milk glass (n=3),
and olive (n=2; pre-1900 [Lindsey 2012]). The bottle lip is from an aqua bottle, but is too small
to discern more than that it was probably a bead lip. The bottle neck is also from an aqua bottle.
Although it is missing the lip, enough remains to indicate that this was a non-applied, turnmolded neck with Packer’s lip (1880–1910). The Architectural artifacts consist of colorless (n=3;
post-1880), light green (n=8), and light-tinted (n=9) window glass, wire nails (n=3; post-1890),
and a piece of slate. The Furnishings-related artifacts are two ash-tint (post-1915) and one
colorless (post-1880) lamp glass fragments. The Activities-related artifacts are a fence staple, a
piece of barbed wire, and a possible piece of safety glass. The barbed wire is composed of a twopointed barb wrapped around a single strand of wire. It is consistent with Glidden's Coil,
Hanging Barb One-Strand Variation or Glidden's Barb, One-Strand Variation (post-1876; Clifton
1970:30–31). The possible safety glass consists of a piece of clear, flat glass filled with internal
bubbles or fractures. The Indeterminate artifacts are amber/brown (n=1), colorless (n=4; post1880), and light green (n=1) angular glass fragments; an iron rod fragment; and a piece of modern
plastic (post-1945). The plastic is probably from some type of vessel, because it is curved and
laminated with thick white plastic on the interior and a thin green veneer on the exterior.
In addition to the historic period component of the site, two prehistoric artifacts were also
recovered from ST C-2. These two artifacts, both small Edwards chert core flakes, indicate the
potential of minimal use of this area at some time during the prehistoric period.

A primary structure is depicted at the location of site 41VT160 on the 1927 USDA Victoria
County 1:63,000-scale soils map and the 1951 USGS Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic
map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the property.
Like site 41VT157, site 41VT160 is located on the west half of the north half of the south half of
the Polito Castillo league, along the west side of Old Bloomington Road. This tract of land was
known as the H. M. Bingham 200-acre homestead in 1881 and was owned by H. M. Bingham, the
widow of Peter Byrne. Between 1881 and 1920, H. M. Bingham granted the property to Ida
Byrne, who maintained ownership of the homestead until her death in 1920. After Ida Byrne’s
death, the property was willed to her niece Elizabeth Woodhouse, who sold the portion of the
tract on the west side of Old Bloomington Road to C. H. Stiernberg and D. D. Spencer (VCC
1920:Probate Minutes [PM] 16:589; VCC 1945:DB 209:56). In 1960, C. H. Stiernberg and D. D.
Spencer sold the property to Joseph B. Bumgardner, who in 1977 sold the property to the Gulf
Oil Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1960:DB 517:673; VCC 1977:DB 943:315). Between
1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito Castillo
league and formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the ownership of
the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC 2012:DB
3914:17).
Site 41VT160 likely represents the location of a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century
farmstead. As with many of the historic period farmsteads in the region, the site may have been
occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner; thus, determining the occupant at a
given period is problematic. Demolition of the associated structure has adversely affected the
site’s integrity of design and materials. Given the site’s fragmentary and redundant artifact
content, coupled with disturbances to the site by the Kinder Morgan pipeline and modern
agriculture, 41VT160 is considered to have little research potential. As such, the site is not likely
to yield any additional information relevant to understanding rural domestic life or community
and regional development in the Guadalupe River valley of the central Texas coastal region.
Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT160 is not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the
criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further
investigations are recommended at site 41VT160 for purposes of developing the proposed
Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

Site 41VT161
Site 41VT161 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century domestic site
located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies a pasture area situated on a
level, featureless portion of the upland coastal plain located adjacent to the now defunct Old
Bloomington Road (Figure 32). Fourteen shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary,
in conjunction with the surface artifact scatter and architectural remains. Five positive shovel
tests occupy the central portion of the pasture. Site 41VT161 encompasses approximately 1.4
acres, measuring approximately 85 m (279 ft) north-to-south by 85 m (279 ft) east-to-west at an
elevation of 19 m (62 ft) amsl. A windmill and a steel stock tank are situated in the central
portion of the site (Figure 33). The ruins of a shed or barn are immediately northwest of the
windmill.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site is situated in an area of Laewest clay. The soils encountered during this
survey confirm this identification.
72

33. Photograph of site 41VT161, view to the northwest

Figure 33. Photograph of site 41VT161, view to the northwest.

Surface artifacts observed at the site, in addition to those observed within piles of debris
previously mentioned, consisted of two domestic artifacts, a piece of undecorated porcelain and a
faceted cobalt blue glass bead . The glass bead, which was collected, is football-shaped with a 1mm diameter hole through the long axis. It measures 20.5 mm in length and 14.5 mm at the
maximum diameter.
The subsurface artifact assemblage encountered at site 41VT161 (n=21) recovered from five
positive shovel test at depths ranging from 0–20 cmbs (0–8 inbs) consists of Domestic (n=12),
Architectural (n=5), Furnishing (n=1), and Indeterminate (n=3) artifacts. The Domestic category
contains four ceramics: blue-tinted, non-vitrified ironstone (1850–1910), pure white whiteware
(post-1890), and two piece of porcelain. The Domestic glass includes three amber/brown, one
clear (post-1880), one light tinted, and one milk glass body sherds. An amber/brown bottle lip
with Packer’s finish was also collected; unfortunately, this artifact is too heavily patinated
(“sick”) to identify whether mold seams were present. Finally, a clear glass (post-1880) basal
fragment was identified solely by its wedge-shaped cross section. The Architectural-related
artifacts are two aqua, one clear (post-1880), and two light tinted window glass fragments. The
Furnishing-related artifact is a piece of clear with ash tint lamp glass (post-1915). The
Indeterminate artifacts are two unidentified pieces of thin iron and a rubber fragment. The rubber
fragment exhibits a deep, broad incised/molded line on the exterior, whereas the interior has a
series of fine, shallowly incised/molded lines. No function is currently ascribed to this artifact.

1980:DB 1068:473). Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several
tracts from the Polito Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that
is now under the ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB
3644:11; VCC 2012:DB 3914:17).

the vicinity, 41VT161 is considered to have little research potential. As such, the site is not likely
to yield any additional information relevant to understanding rural domestic life or community

Site 41VT162
Site 41VT162 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century domestic site
located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site occupies the boundary between an
overgrown pasture and a plowed agricultural field and is situated on a level, featureless portion of
the upland coastal plain located adjacent to the now defunct Old Bloomington Road (Figure 34).
Eleven shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary, in conjunction with the surface
artifact scatter. The one positive shovel test is in the central portion of the site. Site 41VT162
75

encompasses approximately 1.9 acres, measuring approximately 185 m (607 ft) northeast-tosouthwest by 60 m (197 ft) northwest-to-southeast at an elevation of 19 m (62 ft) amsl. A

Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) sandy clay loam to black (10YR 2/1) clay. Based on maps and soil descriptions
prepared by the NRCS (2012), the site occupies the boundary of the Dacosta sandy clay loam and

Surface artifacts observed at the site (n=34) consisted largely of domestic artifacts including

One ash-tint (post-1920) bottle finish exhibited a machine-made, continuous thread screw and a
crown or Goldy closure with a molded bead directly below the continuous thread screw element;
this artifact was also collected.
The subsurface artifact assemblage encountered at site 41VT162 consisted of a single piece of
stoneware with a salt-glazed exterior and a natural clay slipped interior (1840–1900) recovered in
ST C-3 (0–10 cmbs [0–4 inbs]).

Site 41VT163
Site 41VT163 consists of a very low-density, late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century domestic
site located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site is situated on a level, featureless
portion of the upland coastal plain and occupies a portion of a plowed agricultural field located
adjacent to the now defunct Old Bloomington Road (Figure 35). Five shovel tests were
excavated to define the site boundary, in conjunction with the surface artifact scatter. No artifacts
were recovered in any of the shovel tests within or surrounding the site. Site 41VT163
encompasses approximately 0.5 acres, measuring approximately 100 m (328 ft) northeast-tosouthwest by 25 m (82 ft) northwest-to-southeast at an elevation of 19 m (62 ft) amsl.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site occupies the boundary of the Dacosta sandy clay loam and the Laewest
clay. The soils encountered during this survey confirm this identification.
Surface artifacts observed at the site (n=9) consisted largely of historic period domestic artifacts
including undecorated refined earthenware (n=3), undecorated porcelain (n=1), a ceramic tile
(n=1), solarized/manganese glass (n=1; post-1900), and two brick fragments. A single prehistoric
artifact, consisting of a medial fragment of a biface, represents the only prehistoric artifact
associated with the site. This lone prehistoric artifact may represent a hunting loss or an item
collected and brought to the site by its historic period inhabitants.
A structure is depicted at the location of site 41VT163 on the 1927 USDA Victoria County

M. Bingham by the Victoria County Court in 1881. Between 1881 and 1897, H. M. Bingham
granted the property to Ida Byrne, who in 1897 sold Lots One, Two, Three, and Four of Block
One to W. P. Stafford and C. L. Thurmond (VCC 1897:DB 36:516). Between 1897 and 1924, the
property was granted to L. G. Kreisle, who owned the land until his death in 1924. After the
death of L. G. Kreisle, the land was willed to his brother Emil Kreisle of San Antonio (VCC
1924:DB 105:29). Emil Kreisle continued to maintain a permanent residence in San Antonio
until his death in 1945, but according to his obituary, he was devoted to farming and ranching in
his later years (San Antonio Express 17 November 1945). Between 1924 and 1945, Emil Kreisle
granted the property to his daughter Myrtle Kreisle Trip, who in 1974 granted the property to her
son Gustav T. Trip Jr. (VCC 1974:DB 860:476). In 1975, Gustav T. Trip Jr. granted the property
to his cousins Lexina K. Miguez and Lissette Trip Wilson, who in 1977 sold the property to the
Gulf Oil Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1975:DB 869:255; VCC 1977:DB 942:655).
Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito
Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the
ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC
2012:DB 3914:17).

farmstead. As with many of the historic period farmsteads in the region, the site may have been
occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner; thus, determining the precise
occupant at a given period is problematic. Demolition of the associated structures has adversely
affected the site’s integrity of design and materials. Given the site’s minimal and fragmentary
78

artifact content, coupled with disturbances to the site by the current agricultural and ranching

community and regional development in the Guadalupe River valley of the central Texas coastal
region. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT163 is not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP
under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8.

Site 41VT164
Site 41VT164 consists of a low-density, late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century domestic site
located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site is situated on a level, featureless
portion of the upland coastal plain and occupies a portion of an overgrown pasture located
adjacent to McCoy Road (Figure 36). Eleven shovel tests were excavated to define the site
boundary, in conjunction with the surface artifact scatter. One positive shovel test was
encountered in the central portion of the site. Site 41VT164 encompasses approximately 0.4
acres, measuring approximately 55 m (180 ft) north-to-south by 55 m (180 ft) east-to-west at an
elevation of 19 m (62 ft) amsl.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the
NRCS (2012), the site occupies an area of the undifferentiated Dacosta-Contee complex. The
soils encountered during this survey support this identification.
Surface artifacts observed at the site (n=10) consisted largely of historic period domestic artifacts
including undecorated refined earthenware (n=3), a milk glass canning jar lid insert (n=1), thick
plate glass (n=1), milk glass (n=1), and four brick fragments. An additional fragment of
undecorated refined earthenware was observed outside of the project area to the southeast.
The subsurface artifact assemblage from 41VT164 consists of three Domestic artifacts recovered
from a depth of 10 to 30 cmbs (4 to 12 inbs) in ST BL-1. All of the artifacts recovered in the
shovel test are ceramics and include one fragment of blue-tinted, non-vitrified ironstone (1850–
1910), a light blue tinted whiteware (1880–1930), and a pure white whiteware sherd (post-1890).
A structure is depicted at the location of site 41VT164 on the 1927 USDA Victoria County
1:63,000-scale soils map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership
of the property. The site is located on the west half of the south half of the south half of the
Polito Castillo league. In 1881, this portion of land was owned by Winn Traylor, who moved to
Victoria County in 1840, and established himself as ranchman. Winn Traylor lived on the west
side of the property, near the Guadalupe River, until his death in 1882 (Victoria Advocate 12 May
1968b). After the death of Winn Traylor, it is unknown to whom the property was passed, but it
was probably inherited by his heirs. In 1934, Mrs. Lena Mae Traylor, et ux., granted the property
to John W. Maroney, who between 1934 and 1945 granted the property to D. D. Spencer and wife
Opal Spencer (VCC 1945:DB 204:343). Between 1945 and 1972, D. D. Spencer and Opal
Spencer sold the property to Pete Rydolph and his wife Gertrude Rydolph. In 1972, Gertrude
Rydolph granted the property to her daughter Ross Ella Giles, who in 1977 sold the property to
80

the Gulf Oil Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1972:DB 812:823; VCC 1977:DB 955:679).
Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito
Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the
ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC
2012:DB 3914:17).
Site 41VT164 likely represents the location of a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century
farmstead. Like many of the historic period farmsteads in the region, the site may have been
occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner, making a precise determination of the
occupant at a given period problematic. Demolition of the associated structure or structures has
adversely affected the site’s integrity of design and materials. Given the site’s minimal,
fragmentary and redundant artifact content, coupled with disturbances to the site by the current
agricultural and ranching activities in the vicinity, 41VT164 is considered to have little research
potential. As such, the site is not likely to yield any additional information relevant to
understanding rural domestic life or community and regional development in the Guadalupe
River valley of the central Texas coastal region. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT164 is
not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for
designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further investigations are recommended at site
41VT164 for purposes of developing the proposed Industrial Park South, as it is presently
defined.

Site 41VT165
Site 41VT165 consists of a prehistoric habitation site located within proposed Industrial Park
South APE. The site is situated on an upland escarpment above the former channel of Black
Bayou, now an intermittent stream that has been dammed for use as a stock tank. An unnamed
two-track road bisects the site. Thirty-nine shovel tests were excavated to define the site
boundary, in conjunction with the surface artifact scatter (Figure 37). Ten positive shovel tests
were encountered, with the majority clustering around the central portion of the site. Site
41VT165 encompasses approximately 3.3 acres, measuring approximately 140 m (560 ft) northto-south by 150 m (492 ft) east-to-west at an elevation ranging from 11 to 16 m (36 to 52 ft) amsl.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the summit of the landform is mantled by
very dark gray (10YR 3/1) to black (10YR 2/1) clay loam, with dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2)
fine sandy loam occurring on the slope around the summit of the landform. This is nearly
opposite the data provided by the NRCS (2012); current investigations indicate that erosion has
likely removed the sandy mantle that once occupied the summit of the landform and redeposited
it along the adjacent slopes.
Surface artifacts observed at the site (n=33) consisted primarily of chipped lithic debitage (n=28)
exposed within the two-track road bisecting the site. Three surface lithics, all of chert, were
collected from the two-track road at the crest of the knoll: two biface thinning flakes and a small
edge-modified flake with a minimally worked distal edge forming a small concavity. Several
marine and brackish water mollusk shells were also observed and are presumably part of the
prehistoric archeological assemblage. Only two historic periods artifacts, consisting of aqua
bottle glass and a single chain link, also exposed within the two-track road were present.

82

The subsurface artifact assemblage from 41VT165 consists of 17 chipped lithic artifacts, several
fragments of burned clay, and several fragments of burned shell. Of the chipped lithic artifacts,
the majority consists of chipped lithic debitage (n=16); seven are biface thinning flakes and one is
a primary reduction core flake. The remaining eight flake fragments are fragmentary and could
not be classified with more detail. A single chipped lithic tool, a distal fragment of a biface, was
recovered. Unfortunately, the biface is too fragmentary to be temporally or functionally
diagnostic, although it likely was part of a Late Archaic period dart point. The majority of the
artifacts recovered from shovel tests (n=15) at site 41VT165 were encountered in the uppermost
30 cm (12 in), with the exception of two pieces of debitage recovered from 40 to 50 cmbs (16 to
20 inbs) and 60 to 70 cmbs (24 to 28 inbs) in ST CG-11, located on the western slope of the
landform (see Figure 37). Burned clay, burned shell, and charcoal were noted in STs CG-4, CG5, CG-6, and CG-21. With the exception of ST CG-4, all of the shovel tests that exhibited
evidence of midden deposits (i.e., burned clay, burned shell, and charcoal) were located along the
shoulder slope that retains a portion of the loose, fine sandy loam surface soil. It is likely that the
midden deposit has eroded from the summit of the landform. The accumulation of surface
artifacts along the shoulder slope within the two-track road and a series of narrow linear gullies
roughly paralleling the two-track road in the southwestern portion of the site provide
corroborating evidence of slope erosion at the site.
Site 41VT165 represents a prehistoric habitation site of uncertain temporal affiliation. The
cultural deposits at the site have likely lost their original integrity due to erosion of the fine sandy
loam soils at the site. Although some midden-like deposits were encountered on the shoulder
slope of the landform, these deposits are likely to have been eroded from the landform summit.
No temporally diagnostic artifacts were recovered at the site, and no cultural features were
encountered. Given the site’s minimal and fragmentary artifact content, coupled with
disturbances to the site by natural and artificial erosion due to historic and modern agricultural
and ranching activities in the vicinity, 41VT165 unlikely to retain sufficient data to contribute
important information regarding prehistoric technology, settlement-subsistence patterns, or extraregional interaction. As such, the site is considered to have little research potential. The site is
not likely to yield any additional information relevant to understanding the prehistoric period
within the central Texas coastal region. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT165 is not eligible
for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an
SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further investigations are recommended at site 41VT165 for
purposes of developing the proposed Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

Site 41VT166
Site 41VT166 consists of a low-density prehistoric site with a late nineteenth- to early twentiethcentury domestic component located within proposed Industrial Park South APE. The site is
situated on a dissected upland escarpment, likely a High Deweyville terrace (Figure 38).
Currently in pasture, the groundcover consists of a wide array of forbs, grasses, and herbaceous
plants (Figure 39). The site was initially discovered during the survey of the Guadalupe River
valley margin (high probability area [HPA]) when a shovel test (HPA ST CG-12) encountered
bone fragments and a charcoal fragment between 80 and 100 cmbs (32 and 39 inbs). Although
the results of the shovel test are ambiguous (i.e., not definitively cultural in origin and not
temporally diagnostic), additional radial shovel tests were excavated to determine if cultural
deposits were, in fact, present at the location. Radial shovel tests excavated at 20-m intervals in
two orthogonal directions from HPA ST CG-12 encountered both prehistoric and historic
84

39. Photograph of site 41VT166, view to the north

Figure 39. Photograph of site 41VT166, view to the north.

artifacts. Thirty-three shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary (see Figure 38).
No surface artifacts were encountered at the site. Nine additional positive shovel tests were
encountered on the summit and along the eastern shoulder slope of the landform. Site 41VT166
encompasses approximately 1.7 acres, measuring approximately 90 m (295 ft) north-to-south by
103 m (338 ft) east-to-west at an elevation of range of 10 to 14 m (33 to 46 ft) amsl.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the western portion of the site is mantled
by very pale brown (10YR 7/4) loamy fine sand to depths extending to 100 cmbs (40 inbs)
overlying brown (7.5YR 5/4) sandy clay. Ant mound spoils observed in the southern portion of
the site indicate that the deeply buried brown sandy clay Bt horizon is being brought to the
surface by the insects. Soils in the eastern portion of the site consist of dark grayish brown
(10YR 4/2) fine sandy loam extending to approximately 50 cmbs (20 inbs) and overlying very
dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) sandy clay loam in the eastern portion of the site. Based on maps
and soil descriptions prepared by the NRCS (2012), the site occupies the interface between Kuy
loamy sand and Runge fine sandy loam. The soils encountered during this survey support this
identification.
No surface artifacts were observed at the site. The subsurface artifact assemblage from 41VT166
consists of both prehistoric and historic artifacts. Prehistoric artifacts were encountered in nine of
the 10 positive shovel tests, but historic period artifacts were confined to five shovel tests
generally along the summit of the landform (see Figure 38).

86

Prehistoric artifacts recovered from site 41VT166 consist of 25 chipped lithic artifacts. The
dominant artifact class is chipped lithic debitage (n=21), consisting of one blade, eight biface
thinning flakes, one core flake, six fragmentary flakes that could not be classified in more detail,
and five fragments of angular or blocky chipping debris (shatter). In addition, two unifacial tools,
one bifacial knife, and one biface fragment are present in the assemblage. The biface fragment is
a medial portion of a probable arrow point, indicating a Late Prehistoric occupation at the site;
however, the artifact is too fragmentary to be stylistically diagnostic. Prehistoric artifacts were
recovered at depths between 0 and 90 cmbs (0 and 35 inbs) across the site with no significant
concentrations in the vertical profile. The majority of the prehistoric artifacts (n=18) were
recovered from STs CG-9, CG-12, CG-13, and CG-14, which form a transect that traverses the
major axis of the landform (see Figure 38).
Twenty-seven historic period artifacts were collected from site 41VT166, consisting of
Architectural (n=14), Domestic (n=7), Furnishing (n=4), and Indeterminate (n=2) artifacts. The
Thirteen cut nails (1840–1880) and one light green window glass fragment represent the
Architecture-related artifacts recovered from the site. The Domestic category contains four
ceramics: a blue-tinted, high-fired ironstone (1850–1910), a blue-tinted, non-vitrified ironstone
(1850–1910), pure white whiteware (post-1890), and alkaline glazed interior/exterior stoneware
(1840–1900). The three Domestic glass include amber/brown, clear with ash tint (post-1915),
and olive (pre-1900; Lindsey 2012a) body sherds. The Furnishing-related artifacts are three light
tinted and one colorless (post-1880) lamp glass fragments. The Indeterminate artifacts are two
pieces of iron wire. Historic period artifacts were recovered at depths between 10 and 80 cmbs (4
and 32 inbs). The majority of the historic period artifacts were recovered from Level 7 (60–70
cmbs [24–28 inbs]; n=10) and Level 8 (70–80 cmbs [28–32 inbs]; n=6). Historic period artifacts
were encountered across the most level portion of the landform and were concentrated in STs
CG-5 (n=8) and ST CG-9 (n=12).
No structures are depicted at the location of site 41VT166 on either the 1927 USDA Victoria
County 1:63,000-scale soils map or the 1951 USGS Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic
map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the property.
The site is located on the west half of the north half of the north half of the Polito Castillo league,
on a portion of land that was known as Lot One of Block One and granted to Ida Byrne by the
Victoria County Court in 1881. Ida Byrne maintained ownership of the land until 1897, at which
time she sold Lots One, Two, Three, and Four of Block One to W. P. Stafford and C. L.
Thurmond (VCC 1897:DB 36:516). Soon after obtaining the land in 1897, W. P. Stafford and C.
L. Thurmond sold a 321-acre tract to Austin Hickey (VCC 1897:DB 38:623). The 321-acre tract
was located from the east bank of the Guadalupe River to the west side of the Old Bloomington
Road along the north boundary line of the Polito Castillo league with a south boundary line along
Stubblefield Road. Austin Hickey lived on the property and maintained ownership of the 321acre tract until his death in 1903 (Advocate 3 October 1903), at which time the land was passed to
his heirs Tina Hickey, Mary Hickey, Richard Hickey, Andrew Hickey, and Joseph Hickey. In
1905, the Austin Hickey heirs foreclosed on the property and the 321-acre tract was sold in a
Sheriff’s sale to Mary T. Glass, who sold the property to Adolph Fromme in 1906 (VCC
1905:DB 53:186; VCC 1906:DB 56:261). Adolph Fromme is known to have lived on the
property in the “Austin Hickey Place” in ca. 1910, and maintained ownership of the property until
his death in 1943 (Daily Advocate 4 November 1910). In 1943, the property was passed to
Adolph Fromme’s widow Teresa Fromme, who maintained ownership of the property until her
death in 1976 (Advocate 18 January 1976). After the death of Teresa Fromme, the property was
willed to her two surviving sisters Ernestine Ackermann and Elizabeth Ackermann, who in 1980
87

sold the property to the Gulf Oil Corporation of Pennsylvania (VCC 1980:DB 1068:473).
Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation consolidated several tracts from the Polito
Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing 1,955.21 acres that is now under the
ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation (VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC
2012:DB 3914:17).
Site 41VT166 represents a prehistoric habitation site of uncertain temporal affiliation with
evidence of a late nineteenth- to early twentieth-century domestic occupation. The cultural
deposits have likely lost their original integrity due to bioturbation of the deep loamy sands at the
site. This is exemplified by the majority of historic period artifacts occurring below the majority
of the prehistoric artifacts recovered from the site, despite the lack of evidence for historic period
trash pits or privies to explain the mixed deposits. No temporally diagnostic prehistoric artifacts
were recovered at the site, and no cultural features were encountered. The site may have been
occupied by Austin Hickey and/or Adolph Fromme in the late nineteenth century and early
twentieth century, respectively. However, as with many of the historic period farmsteads in the
region, the site may also have been occupied by a tenant farmer rather than the property owner,
making a precise association of occupancy at a given period problematic. Demolition of the
associated structure or structures has adversely affected the site’s integrity of design and
materials. Given the site’s minimal and fragmentary artifact content, coupled with disturbances
to the site by bioturbation and natural and artificial erosion due to historic and modern
agricultural and ranching activities in the vicinity, 41VT166 is unlikely to retain sufficient data to
contribute important information regarding technology, settlement-subsistence patterns, or extraregional interaction during the prehistoric period or rural domestic life and community and
regional development in the Guadalupe River valley or Victoria County during the historic
period. As such, the site is considered to have little research potential. The site is not likely to
yield any additional information relevant to understanding the prehistoric or historic periods
within the central Texas coastal region. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT166 is not eligible
for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for designation as an
SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further investigations are recommended at site 41VT166 for
purposes of developing the proposed Industrial Park South, as it is presently defined.

Site 41VT167
Site 41VT167 consists of a possible low-density prehistoric lithic scatter and a low-density, earlyto-mid-twentieth-century historic activity area located within the proposed Industrial Park South
APE. The site is situated on a level portion of the Guadalupe River floodplain and occupies the
western corner of a pasture located adjacent to Stubblefield Road (Figure 40). Four shovel tests
were excavated in an attempt to define the site boundary, but no subsurface artifacts were
encountered. The site boundary is defined within the surface artifact scatter. Site 41VT167
encompasses approximately 0.6 acres, measuring approximately 70 m (230 ft) north-to-south by
50 m (164 ft) east-to-west at an elevation of 5 m (16 ft) amsl.
Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the site is mantled by very dark gray
(10YR 3/1) clay or clay loam. Based on maps and soil descriptions prepared by the NRCS
(2012), the site occupies an area of Meguin silty clay. The soils encountered during this survey
support this identification.

88

Surface artifacts observed at the site (n=10) consisted of six possible pieces of prehistoric chert
debitage and four historic period domestic artifacts including undecorated refined earthenware
(n=1), stoneware with natural clay slipped interior/exterior (n=1), milk glass (n=1), and aqua
bottle glass (n=1). Identification of the six prehistoric artifacts is tenuous due to the presence of
chert cobbles and gravels within Stubblefield Road. All observed materials at the site were
situated within Stubblefield Road or around the western edge of the adjacent agricultural field.
No artifacts were collected from the site.
No structures are depicted at the location of site 41VT167 on either the 1927 USDA Victoria
County 1:63,000-scale soils map or the 1951 USGS Bloomington 1:24,000-scale topographic
map. Additional archival research was conducted, focusing on the ownership of the property.
The site is located on the west half of the north half of the north half of the Polito Castillo league,
on a portion of land that was known as Lot Five of Block One and granted to Robert Byrne by the
Victoria County Court in 1881. Between 1881 and 1897, Robert Byrne granted the property to
W. P. Stafford and C. L. Thurmond, who sold a 321-acre tract, containing a portion of Lot Five,
to Austin Hickey (VCC 1897:DB 38:623). The 321-acre tract was located from the east bank of
the Guadalupe River to the west side of the Old Bloomington Road along the north boundary line
of the Polito Castillo league with a south boundary line along Stubblefield Road. Austin Hickey
lived on the property and maintained ownership of the 321-acre tract until his death in 1903
(Advocate 3 October 1903), at which time the land was passed to his heirs Tina Hickey, Mary
Hickey, Richard Hickey, Andrew Hickey, and Joseph Hickey. In 1905, the Austin Hickey heirs
foreclosed on the property and the 321-acre tract was sold in a Sheriff’s sale to Mary T. Glass,
who sold the property to Adolph Fromme in 1906 (VCC 1905:DB 53:186; VCC 1906:DB
56:261). Adolph Fromme is known to have lived on the property in the “Austin Hickey Place” in
ca. 1910, and maintained ownership of the property until his death in 1943 (Daily Advocate 4
November 1910). In 1943, the property was passed to Adolph Fromme’s widow Teresa Fromme,
who maintained ownership of the property until her death in 1976 (Advocate 18 January 1976).
After the death of Teresa Fromme, the property was willed to her two surviving sisters Ernestine
Ackermann and Elizabeth Ackermann, who in 1980 sold the property to the Gulf Oil Corporation
of Pennsylvania (VCC 1980:DB 1068:473). Between 1977 and 1980, the Gulf Oil Corporation
consolidated several tracts from the Polito Castillo league and formed one tract of land containing
1,955.21 acres that is now under the ownership of the Victoria County Port Facilities Corporation
(VCC 2007:DB 3644:11; VCC 2012:DB 3914:17).
Interpretation of site function at 41VT167 is problematic. The chert debris accumulated at the
site may have been transported to the location with road surfacing materials, or has been created
by vehicle traffic over chert road surfacing material. The historic period artifacts at the site likely
represent an early-to-mid-twentieth-century activity area related to local agricultural operations,
such as a convenient location to eat lunch while working in the fields. Given the site’s minimal
and redundant artifact content, 41VT167 is considered to have little research potential. As such,
the site is unlikely to retain sufficient data to contribute important information regarding
technology, settlement-subsistence patterns, or extra-regional interaction during the prehistoric
period or rural domestic life and community and regional development in the Guadalupe River
valley or Victoria County during the historic period. Thus, it is recommended that site 41VT167
is not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for
designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. No further investigations are recommended at site
41VT167 for purposes of developing the proposed Industrial Park South, as it is presently
defined.
90

Site 41VT98, revisited
Site 41VT98, also known as the Buckeye Knoll site, consists of a prehistoric habitation site and
associated cemetery located on both sides of the Victoria Barge Canal near its intersection with
DuPont Road. The site was initially discovered in the 1960s by vocational archeologist Ed Vogt
during the construction of the barge canal (Weinstein 1992:271). The site was formally recorded
in 1982 by Carolyn Good during a U.S. Army Corps of Engineers reconnaissance survey of the
barge canal prior to its expansion (Weinstein 1992:274). Due to limited access and the lack of
subsurface testing, Good was not able to fully evaluate the site, but determined that cultural
materials existed on both sides of the barge canal. In 1989, a large portion of the site west of the
barge canal was subjected to extensive excavations to determine the NRHP eligibility of the site
(Weinstein 1992), although the 1989 investigation also included a small portion of the site to the
east of the barge canal (Weinstein 1992:277–283). Investigations east of the barge canal
consisted of 14 auger tests and the excavation of one 1-x-1-m test unit. These investigations
indicated that deposits east of the barge canal differed significantly from those encountered in
excavations west of the barge canal. Excavations west of the barge canal encountered thick
prehistoric midden deposits, a suite of temporally diagnostic artifacts, and abundant faunal
remains, but the excavations east of the barge canal recovered only minor amounts of
nondiagnostic prehistoric lithic debris. Based on the findings of the 1989 excavations, the site
was deemed eligible for inclusion in the NRHP (Weinstein 1992:327). Site 41VT98 was briefly
revisited in 1991 during a pipeline relocation project when additional auger tests and an
additional 1-x-1-m test unit was excavated in the southwestern portion of the site (Weinstein
1991). In 2000 and 2001, data recovery excavations to mitigate the adverse effects related to the
expansion of the Victoria Barge Canal on site 41VT98 were conducted. The site is now
understood to span prehistory from the Late Paleoindian to the Late Prehistoric periods and
preserves one of the earliest and largest prehistoric cemeteries in North America. Seventy-five
burials, primarily dating to the Early Archaic period, were excavated at the site, yielding evidence
a complex mortuary tradition based on individual status and the incorporation of exotic prestige
items with the burials of high status individuals. Excavations at the site provided a wealth of
knowledge regarding the regional prehistoric chronology, evidence of extra-regional trade,
complex mortuary behaviors, and hunter-gatherer subsistence data. Although publication of the
final report of the project is forthcoming (Ricklis 2007), a recent article summarizes the findings
of the investigations (Ricklis 2011).
Site 41VT98 is situated on a dissected upland escarpment extending into the Guadalupe River
valley. The site is bisected by the Victoria Barge Canal. The western portion of the site has been
the focus of archeological mitigation and research in recent decades. Conversely, the portion of
the site east of the barge canal has had relatively little attention from archeologists due to its
apparent lack of dense prehistoric cultural deposits and its location outside of the barge canal
right-of-way. The eastern portion of the site does, however, extend into the proposed Industrial
Park South APE. Therefore, this area of the site was revisited as part of this survey effort.
One hundred fourteen shovel tests were excavated to define the site boundary within the proposed
Industrial Park South APE (Figure 41). Forty-eight positive shovel tests were encountered across
the summits and shoulder slopes of the three contiguous portions of the landform. The eastern
portion of site 41VT98, within the Industrial Park South APE, encompasses approximately 9.2
acres, measuring approximately 190 m (623 ft) north-to-south by 380 m (1,247 ft) east-to-west at
an elevation range of 8 to 15 m (26 to 49 ft) amsl (Figure 42).
91

42. Photograph of site 41VT98, view to the northwest

Figure 42. Photograph of site 41VT98, view to the northwest.

Soil profiles examined during this survey indicate that the portion of the site investigated is
mantled by very pale brown (10YR 7/4) loamy fine sand to depths extending to 100 cmbs (40
inbs), overlying brown (7.5YR 5/4) or reddish yellow (7.5YR 7/8) sandy clay. Ant mound spoils
observed near the eastern margin of the site indicate that the deeply buried reddish yellow sandy
clay Bt horizon is being brought to the surface by the insects. Based on maps and soil
descriptions prepared by the NRCS (2012), the soils at the site are mapped as Kuy loamy sand.
The soils encountered during this survey support this identification, although the northern
(upslope) extension of the nearby Meguin floodplain soils is overestimated in NRCS data.
Two surface artifacts, consisting of chipped lithic debitage found near the eastern margin of the
Victoria Barge Canal, were encountered at the site.
The collected prehistoric artifact assemblage from 41VT166 consists of 247 artifacts encountered
in 48 positive shovel tests and one flake from the general surface. The majority of the artifacts
(n=243) consist of chipped stone debitage recovered from 0–130 cmbs (0–52 inbs). Debitage, in
turn, consists of biface thinning flakes (n=117), core flakes (n=20), blades (n=5), blocky or
angular debris (shatter; n=14), and flake fragments that could not be classified in more detail
(n=87). In addition, three edge-modified unifacial tools and one biface fragment were recovered
from depths ranging from 40 to 110 cmbs (16–43 inbs). The biface fragment is too fragmentary
to be stylistically or temporally diagnostic. The majority of the chipped stone (n=241) was
produced from various cherts. Finally, one half of an intensively burned siliceous concretion,
measuring 33.13 mm in length, 48.1 mm in width, and 17.69 mm in thickness and weighing 29.8
grams, representing a possible hammerstone or mano, was recovered from a depth of 100 to 110
93

cmbs (39 to 43 inbs) in ST CG-4. The prehistoric artifacts recovered at depths between 0 and 130
cmbs (0 and 51 inbs) across the site showed with no significant differences between Levels 3
through 10 (30–100 cmbs [12–39 inbs]) in the vertical profile. Although artifacts were recovered
from depths greater than 100 cmbs (39 inbs), too few shovel tests were excavated to depths
greater than 100 cmbs (n=6) to provide a statistically valid sample. Shovel tests with the largest
amount of recovered artifacts are STs CG-4 and CG-1, with 30 and 31 artifacts, respectively
(Figure 43). Artifact density at the site is concentrated on the summit of the westernmost knoll.

43. Site 41VT98 artifact density map

Figure 43. Site 41VT98 artifact density map.

In addition to the prehistoric material, two pieces of modern translucent plastic sheeting (post1945) were found in SH WH-6 at 80–90 cmbs (32–35 inbs) and likely associated with pipeline
installation south of this shovel test.
The eastern portion of site 41VT98 most likely represents prehistoric hunting loci within the
overall site context. The vast majority of artifacts recovered within the eastern portion of the site
is classified as bifacial thinning flakes, consistent with refurbishing chipped stone tools. No
organic-rich midden deposits, like those encountered on the western portion of the site, appear to
be present within the eastern portion of the site. The cultural deposits within the uppermost 100
cmbs (39 inbs) have likely lost their original integrity due to bioturbation of the deep loamy sands
at the site and by ground-disturbing activities associated with the construction of a buried pipeline
that parallels DuPont Road through a portion of the site (see Figure 41). No temporally
diagnostic prehistoric artifacts were recovered within the eastern portion of the site, and no
cultural features were encountered. The uppermost 100 cmbs (39 inbs) of site 41VT98 is unlikely
94

to retain sufficient data to contribute important information regarding technology, settlementsubsistence patterns, or extra-regional interaction during the prehistoric period due to minimal
artifact content, bioturbation, and industrial disturbances. As such, the shallow portion of the site
is considered to have little research potential and is not likely to yield any additional information
relevant to understanding the prehistoric period within the central Texas coastal region.
However, more well-preserved deposits may exist beyond the depth evaluated by shovel testing.
Based on previous excavation data from the western portion of the site, as well as nearby site
41VT94, significant cultural deposits, including burials and other mortuary remains, may be
preserved at depths that could not be evaluated via shovel testing. Thus, it is recommended that
deep testing via backhoe trenching be conducted to evaluate the potential for deeply buried
deposits at the portion of site 41VT98 within the Industrial Park South APE if this area will be
adversely affected by ground disturbing activities.

Unnamed Cemetery (41VT168)
During the course of these investigations, an unnamed early-to-mid-twentieth-century cemetery
was located within proposed Industrial Park South APE (Figure 44). The cemetery is situated on
the toe slope of the Guadalupe River valley margin and extends to the floodplain, occupying a
pasture adjacent to several large live oaks (Figure 45). The cemetery is delineated by a modern
wire fence and a gate, but is overgrown with thistle and other herbaceous weeds and wildflowers.
Several small fragments of concrete and mortar were observed within the fenced enclosure. One
small mortar fragment was decorated with several marine shells adhered to it. Two wooden
posts, one with attached barbed wire remnants and another exhibiting hinge hardware, were
located and mapped within the fenced enclosure (see Figure 44). These posts are likely to have
either marked the original entrance to the cemetery or served to delineate part of a family plot
within it. One monument, constructed of concrete coated with mortar, is in relatively good
condition and records the name and dates: “ANDREW LEWIS. / BoRN.MAY.13.1867. / DIED.
DEC. 9.1946.” (Figure 46). Archival research indicates that an Anderson Lewis was born in the
nearby town of McFaddin on 17 February 1867, and died on 9 December 1946, in Bloomington.
It is likely that Anderson Lewis was a tenant farmer on the property or one of the associated
properties during the early to mid-twentieth century. Two large masses of rough concrete may
represent additional monuments. The number of additional interments and the identities of those
interred could not be ascertained through archival research.
For purposes of this survey, the cemetery boundary is defined as the modern fenced enclosure.
No excavations took place in the vicinity of the cemetery. No evidence of graves was observed
outside of the fenced enclosure. The unnamed cemetery encompasses approximately 0.7 acres,
and measures approximately 45 m (148 ft) northeast-to-southwest by 133 m (436 ft) northwestto-southeast at an elevation range of 5–8 m (16–26 ft) amsl.
This unnamed cemetery is likely to represent a family or community cemetery, perhaps for
tenants of the surrounding farmsteads, in use at least during the 1940s. Cemeteries are typically
not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP under the criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4 or for
designation as an SAL under 13 TAC 26.8. However, it is recommended that this cemetery
(41VT168) be avoided by ground-disturbing activities associated with the development of the
Industrial Park South.

Isolated Surface Artifacts
Based on systematic surface inspection of the proposed project areas, 13 historic period artifacts
and four prehistoric artifacts were located as isolated surface artifacts. Typically, shovel tests
were excavated at or near the locations of these isolates to determine if additional extensive
deposits were present in these locations. In the absence of additional artifacts, these isolated
surface finds were simply documented by location and content. Although isolated surface
artifacts were typically not collected, but merely documented as to location and content, a single
stoneware fragment from Isolate 3, was collected (Table 4; see Figure 14).

None of the isolated surface artifacts documented during this survey is believed to have the
potential to contribute significant information regarding the history of the area. In the absence of
additional artifacts in more well-preserved contexts, none of these isolated surface finds meets the
criteria for archeological site designation and is therefore not eligible for inclusion in the NRHP
or for designation as an SAL, as per 36 CFR 60.4, 13 TAC 26.5(6), 13 TAC 26.5(32), and 13
TAC 26.8. It is therefore recommended that no further work is necessary at these locations.

98

CHAPTER 6
SUMMARY AND RECOMMENDATIONS

SUMMARY
This cultural resources survey was undertaken to document and assess cultural resources within
the 7.6-acre APE for expansion of the Pickering Basin infrastructure and within the 1,390-acre
APE for the development of proposed the Industrial Park South and to provide adequate and
relevant information for use in the management of cultural resources within the project area.
Information regarding previously recorded archeological sites was gathered through a literature
review as well as a review of the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas online database maintained by
the THC. Prior to field investigations at the proposed project area, a suite of archival sources
including historic aerial photographs and maps was examined to determine the former locations
of historic-age structures within the project area. Due to the variability of archeological site
potential across the survey areas, a survey strategy consisting of systematic shovel testing and
backhoe trenching was employed during this cultural resources survey. Shovel testing was
performed in systematic 100-m intervals across the Guadalupe River valley margin, in 3-acre
intervals across the Pleistocene uplands, in areas where surface artifacts were present, and to
define archeological site boundaries. Systematic backhoe trenching across the Guadalupe River
floodplain was also conducted. In total, 685 shovel tests and 37 backhoe trenches were excavated
within the proposed project areas (see Figure 12). All shovel tests and backhoe trenches were
excavated on property owned by the Port of Victoria. The depths of shovel test excavations were
highly variable across the project area and ranged from 20 to 130 cm (8 to 51 in) in depth.

RECOMMENDATIONS
Thirteen new archeological sites were documented and one previously recorded site, 41VT98,
was revisited during the course of these investigations. Sites 41VT155, 41VT156, 41VT157,
41VT158, 41VT159, 41VT160, 41VT161, 41VT162, 41VT163, 41VT164, 41VT166, and
41VT167 represent or have components that represent late nineteenth- to mid-twentieth-century
domestic sites (Table 5). All of these sites have been adversely affected by demolition and
modern agricultural practices. Furthermore, associating occupancy of these sites with owners and
tenants based on archival information is problematic. The integrity of location, design, materials,

Recommendation

and association is severely compromised at all of these sites. The artifact assemblages associated
with them consist of highly fragmentary items typical of late-nineteenth-to-mid-twentieth-century
domestic refuse. No clearly associated architectural features remain at any of these sites. Given
the limited information potential associated with the historic period components of sites
41VT155, 41VT156, 41VT157, 41VT158, 41VT159, 41VT160, 41VT161, 41VT162, 41VT163,
41VT164, 41VT166, and 41VT167, it is unlikely that they will provide any additional
information relevant to understanding community and regional development in Victoria County
or the Lower Guadalupe River valley during the late nineteenth through early twentieth centuries.
Thus, the historic period components of these sites are recommended to be not eligible for
inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL, under criteria enumerated in 36 CFR 60.4
and 13 TAC 26.8, respectively.
Prehistoric site 41VT165 and the prehistoric components of sites 41VT156, 41VT158, 41VT166,
and 41VT167 all lack assemblages of temporally diagnostic artifacts, isolable components, and
intact stratigraphy and cultural features, or have sufficient data to contribute important
information regarding prehistoric technology, settlement-subsistence patterns, or extra-regional
interaction. Thus, the prehistoric components of these sites are recommended to be not eligible
for inclusion in the NRHP or for designation as an SAL, under criteria enumerated in 36 CFR
60.4 and 13 TAC 26.8, respectively. No further investigations are recommended within the
proposed project area at these sites.
Due to the vertical extent of archeological deposits at site 41VT98, the portion of the site within
the proposed Industrial Park South APE was not completely evaluated. Based on the results of
extensive excavations in other portions of the site, as well as the similarities to nearby site
41VT94, site avoidance is recommended. In the event that site 41VT98 cannot be avoided by
ground-disturbing activities, it is recommended that deep testing be conducted to thoroughly
evaluate the potential for deeply buried, significant archeological deposits at the site prior to
ground-disturbing activities.
Due to the sensitive nature of historic period graves and human remains, it is recommended that
site 41VT168 be avoided by ground-disturbing activities.
All artifacts collected and records generated by this project will be curated at the Museum of the
Coastal Bend in Victoria, Texas, at the conclusion of the project.
